<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.23622_figures/2605.23622_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Distribution of |zmax| (the non-trivial eigenvalue of ΦM( ˆU) with largest magnitude) for an ensemble of 10000 Haar-random unitaries ˆU. Peripheral eigenvalues (|zmax| = 1) are extremely rare. The inset shows the mean of each distri- bution as a function of M, which saturates at a value smaller than unity as M increases.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.23622_figures/2605.23622_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Numerical optimization of the nonlocal parameters, characterizing the two-qubit unitary, for the peripheral eigen- values of the map Φ2 composed of two qubit unitary. The conjecture states that Jα = π</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper identifies that the ability to transfer information without loss through a quantum circuit depends on the presence of peripheral eigenvalues in the evolution channel. While dual-unitarity is required for simple qubit chains, larger subsystems or higher-dimensional qudits can achieve lossless transfer even in chaotic, thermalizing regimes.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a surprising link between the existence of non-dissipative information channels and quantum chaos, showing that thermalizing, non-integrable systems can still support perfect information transfer, which is highly relevant for quantum communication and remote sensing in many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the conditions for lossless versus lossy information transfer and remote sensing of parameters along the causal lightcone of a brickwork quantum circuit.

**Main result.** Lossless information transfer is linked to the existence of peripheral eigenvalues (unit modulus) in the quantum channel, a requirement that is necessary for qubits (M=1) but can be relaxed for larger subsystems or higher-dimensional qudits, even in chaotic, thermalizing systems.

**Method.** The authors use a quantum channel framework ($\Phi_M$) to map many-body dynamics to the lightcone, employing spectral analysis of the channel, numerical optimization, and analytical constructions using the Poincaré recurrence theorem and Weyl's inequality.

**Model / system.** A one-dimensional open chain of $N$ qudits (qubits or qutrits) undergoing Floquet dynamics driven by a brickwork quantum circuit of alternating unitary layers.

**Key observables.** Trace distance (distinguishability), Quantum Fisher Information (QFI), information transfer ratios ($\eta_D, \eta_F$), and operator linear entropy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Subsystem size $M$, local dimension $d$, non-local gate parameters ($J_x, J_y, J_z$), and the presence of peripheral eigenvalues ($|z|=1$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is assumed to be in a high-temperature/maximally mixed state outside the encoded subsystem, and the dynamics are restricted to the brickwork circuit class.

**Figures summary.** Schematics of information encoding/recovery; plots of eigenvalue spectra and information decay rates; numerical optimization of gate parameters; and entanglement entropy analysis showing an absence of many-body scars.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of information propagation, defines the brickwork circuit and quantum channel framework, analyzes the spectral conditions for information loss/preservation, extends the findings to larger subsystems and higher dimensions, and concludes by demonstrating that these lossless properties can coexist with quantum chaos and ETH.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding how local information propagates through many-body quantum systems is a central problem in nonequilibrium dynamics, with important implications for quantum communication, state transfer, and remote sensing. In this work, we investigate information transfer along a one-dimensional open chain of qudits, focusing on the task of recovering information initially encoded at one end via measurements performed at the opposite end. By restricting the dynamics to brickwork quantum circuits, and considering small $M$-qudit subsystems on the causal ``lightcone'' of the circuit, we obtain several results valid even for large system sizes $N$ or for nonintegrable global dynamics. Within this framework, lossless information transfer is linked to the existence of peripheral eigenvalues of a quantum channel $Φ_M$, which governs the evolution of the $M$-qudit local subsystem along the lightcone. We investigate conditions under which brickwork circuits admit such peripheral eigenvalues. For qubit chains and $M=1$, we show that the dual-unitary property is necessary, whereas for larger local subsystems ($M \geq 2$) or higher-dimensional qudits, this requirement can be relaxed. Perhaps surprisingly, we can use the peripheral eigenvalue condition to construct examples exhibiting lossless information transfer through chains of arbitrary system size $N$, even when the underlying circuit dynamics is nonintegrable and thermalising at long times.

</details>

### [Unified resonant-manifold framework for dynamical quantum phase transitions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22915v1)

**Authors:** Jesse J. Osborne, Cheuk Yiu Wong, Jad C. Halimeh

As we approach (d), the dynamics of the Z2 LGT becomes that of the spin-1/2 U(1) QLM, quenching from the charge- proliferated (CP) and vacuum (vac) initial states (the positive vacuum is shown in the diagram). Black (white) circles repre- sent occupied (empty) matter sites [and...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22915_figures/2605.22915_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. The evolution of the return rate (top) and particle number difference (bottom) of fully polarized/staggered-link state quenched to µ = h = 0 in the Z2 (1). At this point, the model is equivalent to the free-fermion model (4).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22915_figures/2605.22915_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. A schematic energy diagram of the different product- state configurations considered in the Z2 LGT, which forms the central organizing principle of this work. At µ = h = 0, all configurations are degenerate in energy, while adding a finite h separates the energies of the fully polarized states from the staggered-link states. In the mass-resonance regime µ = h, one of the fully polarized states is brought into resonance with the two staggered-link configurations, while the other remains energetically detuned.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22915_figures/2605.22915_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. The evolution of the return rate (top panels) and electric flux and particle number difference (bottom panels) following the quench of the (a,c) fully polarized and (b,d) staggered-link initial states [illustrated in the upper right corner of the upper panel in (a) and (b), respectively] in the Z2 LGT to µ = 0 and (a,b) h = 0.5J and (c,d) h = J, obtained using infinite matrix product state numerics. We plot the first and second return rates with respect to the initial state λ+ 1,2 (blue curves), and its partner with flipped gauge sites λ− 1,2 (red curves), so that branch dynamical quantum phase transitions (DQPTs) occur when there is a crossing the two lowest return rates which have...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22915_figures/2605.22915_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. The evolution of the return rate (top panels) and electric flux and particle number difference (bottom panels) following the quench of the (a,c) fully polarized and (b,d) staggered-link initial states [illustrated in the upper right corner of the upper panel in (a) and (b), respectively] in the Z2 LGT at mass resonance to (a,b) µ = h = 0.75J and (c,d) µ = h = 2J.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new framework to unify different types of dynamical quantum phase transitions by focusing on energy resonances between state manifolds. By studying a 1+1D lattice gauge theory, the authors show that the nature of these transitions depends on the multiplicity and symmetry of transitional states. This provides a way to use DQPTs as probes for the resonant connectivity of constrained quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental mechanism for classifying non-equilibrium phase transitions based on energy structure and connectivity, which is highly relevant for understanding many-body dynamics and out-of-equilibrium criticality.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of a comprehensive understanding of why different types of dynamical quantum phase transitions (DQPTs), such as regular vs. anomalous and manifold vs. branch, occur in specific many-body scenarios.

**Main result.** The authors propose a unified resonant-manifold framework where manifold DQPTs are driven by resonances within the initial state manifold, while branch DQPTs are driven by resonances with a transitional manifold of states.

**Method.** The study uses infinite Matrix Product State (iMPS) simulations with the Time-Dependent Variational Principle (TDVP) and spectral analysis of energy resonances.

**Model / system.** A 1+1D Z2 Lattice Gauge Theory (LGT) featuring dynamical matter fields, including terms for particle hopping (J), staggered fermion mass (mu), and external electric field strength (h).

**Key observables.** Fidelity (return rate to the initial state), electric flux, and particle number difference.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fermionic mass (mu), electric field strength (h), hopping strength (J), and a bias term (delta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study focuses on the mass-resonance regime and assumes the dynamics can be captured by the connectivity of product-state manifolds in a constrained Hilbert space.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 maps the parameter space of the Z2 LGT; Figure 4 shows the evolution of return rates and local observables for different electric field strengths; Figure 8 displays quench dynamics for fully polarized and staggered-link states.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of DQPT classification, proposes the resonant-manifold framework, applies it to the Z2 LGT model, analyzes specific regimes (mass-resonance, large-h, and U(1) limit), and discusses the implications for understanding resonant connectivity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dynamical quantum phase transitions (DQPTs) are an exciting paradigm of out-of-equilibrium criticality in many-body systems manifested in nonanalytic behavior in the return rate to the initial state following a sudden quench. While previous work has tried to distinguish between distinct types of DQPTs, such as regular and anomalous, or manifold and branch, a comprehensive understanding of why each type appears in a given scenario is still lacking. In this work, we propose a unified framework addressing this gap in terms of the energy structure of different product state configurations. In particular, while manifold DQPTs are governed by resonances within the initial state manifold, branch DQPTs are governed by resonances with a transitional manifold of states dynamically connected to the initial manifold by low-order processes. We show that the (ir)regularity of branch DQPTs is related to the multiplicity of this transitional manifold, and we also observe exotic periods of extended degeneracy in the return rate (beyond the conventional level crossing of a DQPT) which are also conditioned on the structure of this transitional manifold. We demonstrate this by studying quenches of two different configurations in the 1 + 1D Z_2 LGT to various parameter regimes. Our findings provide a dynamical mechanism underlying branch DQPTs and frames DQPTs as probes of resonant connectivity in constrained Hilbert spaces, paving the way to a more complete understanding of the multifaceted nature of dynamical criticality.

</details>

### [Anomalous Decay of Quantum Resources: The Entanglement Sudden Death Mpemba Effect](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23197v1)

**Authors:** Zhilong Liu, Zehua Tian, Jieci Wang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23197v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23197_figures/2605.23197_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic representation of the dissipative model. A bipar- tite system, consisting of two-level subsystems A and B, is initially prepared in an entangled state and subsequently undergoes local dissi- pation through independent zero-temperature reservoirs. Each system undergoes an amplitude damping process with a decay rate of ΓA and ΓB, respectively. Both systems are coupled to their own independent reservoirs maintained at zero temperature (T = 0).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.23197_figures/2605.23197_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Decay of concurrence under symmetric dissipation ΓA = ΓB = 1. (a) Normal concurrence decay for three different initial states: d(0) = 0.4, 0.4, 0.6 and w(0) = 0.35, 0.25, 0.25, respectively. (b) Anomalous concurrence decay for two different initial states: d1(0) = 0.4, w1(0) = 0.25, and d2(0) = 0.8, w2(0) = 0.35. The yellow circle marks the crossing point at (0.29, 0.22) (coordinates rounded to two decimal places).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.23197_figures/2605.23197_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Phase diagrams: solid black semicircle is physical boundary (positivity condition). Grey area: no ESD. Green gradient: quantum Mpemba crossing. Yellow: ESD without Mpemba effect. Blue dot marks reference parameters (|w1(0)| = 0.4, d1(0) = 0.25). Panels: (a) symmetric dissipation (ΓA = ΓB = 1), (b) asymmetric dissipation (ΓA = 0.1, ΓB = 10).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.23197_figures/2605.23197_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. (a) Normal concurrence decay for four different initial states: ΓA=0.1(pink circle), 0.5(blue star), 1(black square), 5(orange triangle), respectively. w(0) = 0.35, d(0) = 0.4. (b) The Mpemba crossing of concurrence Ccross (pink circles) and the corresponding crossing time τcross (blue squares) vary with ΓB. The initial state parameters are fixed as: d1(0) = 0.4, w1(0) = 0.25 and d2(0) = 0.8, w2(0) = 0.35.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.23197_figures/2605.23197_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Characteristic timescales and their dimensionless ratios vary as the ratio ΓB/ΓA. The black dashed line (squares) represents the con- currence trajectory crossing time τcross. Orange dot-dashed line (down- ward triangles) and purple dotted line (diamonds) show ESD times τ∗ ESD1 and τ∗ ESD2 for two distinct initial states. Pink solid line (circles) and blue solid line (stars) give the corresponding ratios τcross/τ∗ ESD1 and τcross/τ∗ ESD2. Vertical grey dotted line marks symmetric dissipation (ΓB = ΓA).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper discovers a quantum version of the Mpemba effect where higher initial entanglement leads to faster entanglement sudden death. It provides an analytical framework to understand this anomalous decay and proves that the effect's existence is robust even when the dissipation rates of the two qubits are unequal.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides new insights into the control of quantum resource lifetimes in dissipative environments and shows how environmental asymmetry affects the dynamics of entanglement decay.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the 'Entanglement Sudden Death (ESD) Mpemba Effect,' where a more strongly entangled initial state decays to a separable state faster than a more weakly entangled one.

**Main result.** The authors analytically derive the conditions for trajectory crossing and demonstrate that the boundaries of this anomalous Mpemba region are robust against environmental dissipation asymmetry.

**Method.** The study uses an analytical approach involving the Lindblad master equation, exact derivation of density matrix elements for X-states, and phase diagram mapping.

**Model / system.** Two independent two-level subsystems (qubits) interacting with local, zero-temperature bosonic reservoirs via pure amplitude damping (spontaneous emission) described by a Lindblad master equation.

**Key observables.** Concurrence (as a measure of entanglement), ESD time (tau*ESD), and crossing time (tau_cross).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Decay rates (Gamma_A, Gamma_B), initial state parameters (w(0), d(0)), and the dissipation asymmetry ratio (Gamma_B/Gamma_A).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Born-Markov and rotating-wave approximations are used; the environment is at zero temperature; the initial states are restricted to a specific class of X-states.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the dissipative model; Fig 2 compares normal decay vs. Mpemba crossing; Fig 3 shows the impact of asymmetric dissipation; Fig 5 plots characteristic timescales and their ratios as functions of the dissipation ratio.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Mpemba effect analogy, defines the two-qubit dissipative model, provides analytical derivations for ESD and crossing times, analyzes the impact of dissipation asymmetry, and concludes with a phase diagram of the effect's boundaries.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In classical thermodynamics, the Mpemba effect refers to the counterintuitive observation that hot water can freeze faster than cold water, manifesting as an anomalous crossing of dynamical trajectories. While analogues of this phenomenon have been explored in quantum radiative systems and spin-chain entanglement asymmetry, its connection to the finite-time decoupling of quantum correlations remains elusive. In this Letter, we uncover a distinct quantum Mpemba effect associated with entanglement sudden death (ESD). By analyzing two qubits interacting with local amplitude damping reservoirs, we demonstrate that a more strongly entangled initial state can experience a faster collapse into a separable state than a more weakly entangled one. We provide an exact analytical derivation of the trajectory crossover dynamics and the ESD time. Finally, we map the phase diagram of initial state parameters to delineate the regime where this anomalous entanglement Mpemba effect occurs, offering insights into the control of quantum resource lifetimes in dissipative environments.

</details>

### [Quantum Simulation of Energy Bifurcation and Z_2-Symmetry Restoration in Macroscopic Quantum Tunneling](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23413v1)

**Authors:** Masao Hirokawa  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23413v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `analog quantum simulation` **4/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **3/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper shows that the Quantum Rabi Model can be used to simulate macroscopic quantum tunneling and the restoration of Z2-symmetry. By mapping the model to a double-well potential, the author demonstrates how instanton-like effects drive energy bifurcation. This provides a potential experimental route to probe non-perturbative physics using quantum simulators.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete bridge between light-matter interaction models (QRM) and fundamental non-perturbative phenomena like instantons and supersymmetry, offering a platform for simulating gauge theory-like physics in cavity QED.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the difficulty of experimentally verifying Macroscopic Quantum Tunneling (MQT) and its connection to instanton physics and Polyakov's energy bifurcation.

**Main result.** The author demonstrates that the Quantum Rabi Model (QRM) can serve as a quantum simulator for instanton-like effects, showing that tunneling restores Z2-symmetry and lifts ground-state degeneracy.

**Method.** The study uses unitary transformations to map the QRM onto an effective double-well potential and employs semiclassical approximations and Euclidean action calculations.

**Model / system.** The primary model is the Quantum Rabi Model (QRM), consisting of a two-level system (qubit) interacting with a single-mode boson (oscillator) with parity symmetry.

**Key observables.** Energy level splitting (E1 - E0), energy gap, and the classical Euclidean action of an effective instanton-like particle.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Qubit transition frequency (wa), boson frequency (wc), coupling strength (g), and the strong-coupling limit (g -> infinity).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The quantum motion in each potential well is assumed to be semiclassical, and the potential near minima is approximated as a harmonic oscillator.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates symmetry breaking and restoration via instantons; Figure 2 shows the double-well potential; Figures 5 and 8 demonstrate the rapid decay of tunneling in specific mathematical limits.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by establishing the MQT/double-well analogy, introduces a transformed QRM to reveal tunneling behavior, analyzes symmetry breaking/restoration via energy bifurcation, and concludes by discussing the implications for simulating complex field theories.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Macroscopic quantum tunneling (MQT), a cornerstone of Leggett's program, is deeply linked with instanton physics, yet its experimental verification remains elusive. This Perspective demonstrates that the quantum Rabi model manifests observable, instanton-like effects via quantum simulation. In the MQT regime, qubit-boson interactions drive Polyakov's energy bifurcation, governing tunneling and spontaneous symmetry breaking. Mapping the quantum Rabi model onto an effective double-well potential reveals that while tunneling suppression induces spontaneous symmetry breaking, instanton-like contributions act to restore it. This mechanism enables experimental access to the classical Euclidean action of an effective instanton-like particle, offering a route to probe non-perturbative phenomena.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.22920"></a>
### [Estimating Green's functions with a robust quantum Arnoldi method](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22920v1)

**Authors:** Jacob S. Nelson, Andrew B. Baczewski  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22920v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22920_figures/2605.22920_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. In the quantum Arnoldi method (ROQAM) a quantum computer is used to estimate expectation values ⟨χ0|U l|χ0⟩ for l = 1, 2, ..., r. These estimates are passed to a classical processor that builds [U]. This matrix is processed into [H], a representation of the Hamiltonian in the Arnoldi basis, which can be used to estimate expectation values of functions of [H].</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22920_figures/2605.22920_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Estimating the Green’s function of a SIAM with 4 bath sites using the ROQAM. In a) we show the spectral function obtained from performing ROQAM with iteration depth 1, 2, and 3, and the exact spectral function. In each case the broadening parameter, γ, is set to 0.1 times the Hubbard bandwidth. In b) we show the mean relative error versus iteration depth for the ROQAM with and without finite-precision errors. We tested the ROQAM with three different levels of initial sampling precision (δ1) and find that the noisy estimates follow the noiseless error curves until hitting a noise floor where increasing the iteration depth no longer improves the error.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22920_figures/2605.22920_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Aggregate T-gate count required to estimate the spectral function of the SIAM with 1 – 5 bath sites to be- low 1% mean relative error using the ROQAM and QSVT. For QSVT, we report estimates for Nω = 100 and Nω = 1000 equally spaced frequencies, and the single frequency with the highest T count. The ROQAM is multiple orders of magni- tude cheaper than the single hardest QSVT and it produces a converged estimate over the entire frequency interval.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22920_figures/2605.22920_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Estimates of the spectral function of SIAM with nbath = 4 with γ = 0.1 (top), 0.01 (middle) and 0.001 (bottom) in units of the Hubbard bandwidth. Each iterate adds 2 poles to the estimate of A(ω, γ). When the poles of A(ω, γ) are clustered near each other, increasing γ will combine these poles, requiring fewer iterates of the ROQAM to well approximate</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22920_figures/2605.22920_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. The estimates Im( ˆG(iω)) on the imaginary frequency axis (denoted iω). Because the poles of the Green’s function lie solely on the real frequency axis G(iω) is always smooth and continuous without the need for a broadening parameter. We see that after only 3 iterations Im( ˆG(iω)) begin to well approximate Im(G(iω)).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces the Robust Quantum Arnoldi Method (ROQAM), a new quantum algorithm for computing Green's functions over entire frequency intervals. By using the time-evolution operator and orthogonal polynomials, the method achieves significant reductions in computational cost compared to existing techniques like QSVT. It is particularly effective for studying the spectral properties of strongly correlated models like the Anderson impurity model at both zero and finite temperatures.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for many-body dynamics as it provides a scalable quantum algorithm for computing spectral properties and Green's functions, which are fundamental to understanding the dynamics of correlated electron systems and open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Efficiently estimating single-particle Green's functions over intervals or multiple points on a quantum computer while overcoming the exponential growth of subnormalization factors and sensitivity to finite-precision errors.

**Main result.** The proposed Robust Quantum Arnoldi Method (ROQAM) provides a functional representation of Green's functions and outperforms Quantum Singular Value Transformation (QSVT) by multiple orders of magnitude in T-gate count for small-scale systems.

**Method.** A quantum adaptation of the Arnoldi method using orthogonal polynomials and the time-evolution operator U(dt) instead of the Hamiltonian directly to preserve the upper-Hessenberg structure and avoid subnormalization scaling.

**Model / system.** The method is demonstrated using the Single-Impurity Anderson Model (SIAM) in both zero-temperature (ground state) and non-zero temperature (Thermofield Double state) regimes.

**Key observables.** Single-particle Green's functions G(omega), spectral functions A(omega), and energy eigenvalues.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Krylov subspace dimension (r), time-step (dt), broadening parameter (gamma), subnormalization factor (lambda), and estimation precision (delta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes efficient access to a block-encoding of the Hamiltonian and that the initial state preparation is possible; neglects identity terms in the Jordan-Wigner transformation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the ROQAM process schematic; Figure 2 displays spectral function estimates and error vs. iteration depth; Figure 3 compares T-gate counts between ROQAM and QSVT; Figure 4-7 show spectral function convergence and error behavior across different parameters; Figure 10-11 illustrate error scaling with subnormalization and error budget strategies.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the ROQAM algorithm, details the mathematical framework using orthogonal polynomials and unitary evolution, extends the method to finite temperatures via the TFD formalism, provides numerical validation using the SIAM, and concludes with resource estimation and error budget analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Many applications of Green's functions (GFs) require their evaluation over intervals or at multiple points, motivating quantum algorithms that return an efficiently computable functional representation rather than mere point estimates. We introduce a robust quantum Arnoldi method (ROQAM) that achieves this goal. Its robustness is derived from formulation in terms of orthogonal polynomials, which preserves the upper-Hessenberg structure of the projected matrices despite finite-precision estimation. We also show that as the iteration depth increases, the precision required for matrix-element estimation can be reduced. Resource estimates for the spectral function of a quantum impurity model indicate that ROQAM outperforms pointwise estimation via quantum singular value transformation by multiple orders of magnitude. Finally, we show that the ROQAM can be used to estimate GFs at nonzero temperatures using only a single Krylov subspace.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22906"></a>
### [Weak wave turbulence as a precursor to universal coarsening in a homogeneous Bose gas](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22906v1)

**Authors:** Simon M. Fischer, Martin Gazo, Sebastian J. Morris, Nikolai Maslov, Haoyu Zhang, Jiří Etrych, Gevorg Martirosyan, Christoph Eigen, Zoran Hadzibabic

Here, we study the initial particle transport, to ≈kξ, and show that it corresponds to...</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>FIG. 2. Emergence of the WWT power-law spectrum. (a) Momentum distribution nk for various relaxation times, corresponding to different kp, for n ≈2.8µm−3 and a = 50a0, so kξ ≈0.43µm−1; k0 ≈1.9µm−1 is the initial value of kp. Once kp drops to ≈kξ, in the spectral range kξ ≲k ≲k0 we observe a power-law nk ∝k−γ, with γ ≈2.4 (red dashed line), close to theoretical predictions (see text). (b) Fitted γ values and the corresponding χ2 (with the dot-dashed line showing χ2 = 1) for fits in two different spectral ranges: from kξ to k0 (open symbols) and from the instantaneous kp to k0 (solid symbols). In the former case, the fitted γ approaches 2.4 (horizontal dashed line) as kp approaches kξ...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22906_figures/2605.22906_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Characteristic particle-transport time. For different n and a (as in Fig. 2), we extract the time ∆t(n,a) for kp to drop from k0 ≈1.9µm−1 to ≈k0/2. (a) Nk normalized by the total atom number N, for kp ≈k0/2 and different (n,a) (colored symbols), and for our initial state (gray); the dashed line indicates the largest kξ. The fact that, as kp decreases, the Nk/N curves for different (n,a) remain the same shows that ∆t(n,a) fully captures the de- pendence of the dynamics on the interactions. Importantly, ∆t is the same (within errors) for the two systems with the same product na (triangles). (b) Plotting ∆t versus na, we find ∆t ∝(na)−2.0(1)</sub>

<sub>FIG. 4. Connection to universal coarsening. In WWT, dk−2 p /dt is ∝(na)2 ∝k4 ξ. However, for kp ≪kξ, in the coarsening regime, it should be bounded by the universal speed limit, ≈0.37ℏ/m (see text), where m is the atom mass. (a) Here we first prepare a gas with n ≈3.7µm−3 and a = 90a0 in a state with kp ≈0.4µm−1, by evolving it from our initial state with kp ≈1.9µm−1, and then change a to different values in the range (7–240)a0, such that kξ varies from ≈0.19µm−1 to ≈1.1 µm−1 (dashed lines). (b) Ex- tracting dk−2 p /dt after the change of a and plotting it versus kp/kξ, we see how it bends away from the WWT scaling (dashed line) to- wards its speed limit (solid line).</sub>

**Summary.** This study experimentally demonstrates that the initial relaxation of a far-from-equilibrium Bose gas is governed by Weak Wave Turbulence. It shows that particle transport follows a universal power-law distribution and scaling laws that act as a precursor to the later stage of universal coarsening.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper provides a direct experimental link between the kinetic regime of weak wave turbulence and the late-time universal coarsening dynamics, offering a complete picture of non-equilibrium many-body relaxation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the relaxation and condensation dynamics of a far-from-equilibrium, homogeneous Bose gas, specifically identifying the universal description of the initial stage of particle transport that precedes universal coarsening.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrate that the initial stage of particle transport follows a universal Weak Wave Turbulence (WWT) description, characterized by a power-law momentum distribution with exponent gamma = 2.4(1) and transport times scaling as (na)^-2.

**Method.** The study uses an experimental platform of 39K atoms in an optical box trap, evolving the gas by tuning the s-wave scattering length via Feshbach resonance and monitoring the time-dependent momentum distribution.

**Model / system.** A homogeneous, low-energy, far-from-equilibrium Bose gas of 39K atoms held in a cylindrical optical box trap with tunable density and interaction strength.

**Key observables.** Spectral population density N_k(k), momentum distribution n_k, peak momentum k_p, and the exponent gamma.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Density n, s-wave scattering length a, healing length xi, and the WWT vs. coarsening regimes.

**Figures summary.** Fig 3(a) shows identical N_k/N curves for different n and a; Fig 3(b) plots transport time delta t versus na; Fig 4(a) shows the evolution of the gas momentum distribution.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Bose gas relaxation, describes the experimental setup with 39K atoms, presents the observation of WWT scaling in momentum distributions and transport times, and concludes by showing the transition from WWT to the universal coarsening regime.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Relaxation and condensation of an isolated low-energy Bose gas provide an ideal setting for the study of the universal features of far-from-equilibrium many-body dynamics and the emergence of long-range order. Conceptually, the emergence of such order involves two steps: the formation of local coherence, on a system-specific microscopic lengthscale, and the spreading of coherence, over lengthscales much larger than any microscopic scale. The latter is understood in terms of universal phase-ordering kinetics, or coarsening, characterized by an algebraic growth of the coherence length. Here, for a homogeneous Bose gas with tunable interactions, we show that the former also has a universal description, within the framework of weak wave turbulence (WWT). Specifically, the initial transport of particles to low momenta corresponds to an inverse turbulent cascade that is, in agreement with the WWT theory, characterized by a power-law momentum distribution, with exponent $γ= 2.4(1)$, and transport times ${\propto} (na)^{-2}$, where $n$ is the gas density and $a$ the $s$-wave scattering length.

</details>

### [Quantum resource redistribution drives spectral splits in dense neutrino gases](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23584v1)

**Authors:** Michael Hite, Pooja Siwach  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23584v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5**

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the emergence of spectral splits in dense neutrino gases is driven by a specific redistribution of entanglement and non-local magic. By mapping these dynamics in a quantum resource phase space, the authors show that splits occur at the intersection of maximal entanglement and minimal non-local complexity. These findings provide a roadmap for optimizing both classical tensor network and future quantum circuit simulations of neutrino environments.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a quantitative link between abstract quantum information measures (like magic and entanglement) and observable astrophysical phenomena, offering insights into how complexity landscapes dictate the efficiency of many-body simulations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how the redistribution of quantum resources, specifically entanglement and non-local magic, drives the emergence of spectral splits in dense neutrino gases.

**Main result.** Spectral splits arise from a structured redistribution of quantum resources where bipartite entanglement is maximized and non-local magic is locally minimized, rather than from generic complexity growth.

**Method.** The authors use tensor network simulations, specifically Matrix Product States (MPS) with the Time-Dependent Variational Principle (TDVP) and Global Subspace Expansion (GSE).

**Model / system.** A dense, two-flavor N-neutrino system modeled by an all-to-all spin Hamiltonian in the mass basis, representing neutrino-neutrino forward scattering in core-collapse supernovae.

**Key observables.** Entanglement entropy, non-local magic (non-stabilizerness), MPS bond dimension, spectral splits (flavor swaps), and antiflatness.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Neutrino-neutrino interaction strength (mu), vacuum oscillation frequency (omega), system size (N=8, 12, 14), and initial flavor configurations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study focuses on the two-flavor sector and utilizes bond dimension truncation as a proxy for computational complexity.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the dynamics of entanglement and magic, trajectories in the entanglement-magic phase space, the relationship between resource metrics and bond dimension, and asymptotic values of flavor survival probability across different system sizes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of resource organization, describes the neutrino Hamiltonian and tensor network methods, presents results on resource redistribution and phase space dynamics, and concludes with implications for classical and quantum simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Whether a quantum many-body system can be efficiently simulated hinges not only on its size but also on how quantum resources are organized within it. We characterize the quantum resource landscape of collective neutrino oscillations using entanglement entropy, non-local magic, and matrix product state bond dimension. Using tensor network simulations of neutrinos in the two-flavor sector, we demonstrate that spectral splits--sharp energy-dependent flavor swaps--emerge precisely where entanglement entropy is maximized and non-local magic is minimized locally. This anticorrelation reveals that spectral splits arise not from generic resource growth but from a structured redistribution among flavor modes. The resource dynamics trace constrained arcs in the entanglement-magic phase space, bounded by entanglement spectrum normalization. These findings establish a direct, quantitative link between quantum resources governing computational complexity and astrophysical observables, informing the design of tensor network and quantum circuit simulations of dense neutrino environments.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.23358"></a>
### [A Compilation Framework for Quantum Simulation of Non-unitary Dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23358v1)

**Authors:** Qifan Huang, Minbo Gao, Li Zhou, Mingsheng Ying  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23358v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23358_figures/2605.23358_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.23358_figures/2605.23358_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 7. Two optimization targets in the LCU implementation pipeline: (a) the channel-level multiplexor over Kraus block-encodings, and (b) the block-encoding-level multiplexor over Pauli terms.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.23358_figures/2605.23358_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 8. Flattening a 3- controlled gate.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.23358_figures/2605.23358_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 9. A channel-level SELECTC oracle with 8 Kraus operators after optimization by conditional flattening (unary iteration).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.23358_figures/2605.23358_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 10. A sketched workflow of Technique II. The first stage assigns Pauli strings into low-cost control addresses by exploiting symplectic structure, while the second stage reconstructs a monotone-control decom- position and the associated phase information for the final implementation.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new compilation framework that treats quantum channels as primary objects rather than decomposing them into unitaries prematurely. By using a specialized intermediate representation, it enables algebraic optimizations that drastically reduce the gate and ancilla overhead for simulating open quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a specialized compilation infrastructure for simulating dissipative and decohering processes, which is essential for modeling realistic many-body dynamics and open quantum systems on near-term and fault-tolerant hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing quantum compilers are optimized for unitary (closed-system) circuits, making them inefficient for simulating open-system dynamics where non-unitary quantum channels are the natural objects.

**Main result.** The proposed ChannelIR framework achieves up to a 99% reduction in gate count and significantly improves compilation scalability compared to traditional circuit-first Stinespring dilation methods.

**Method.** A channel-first compilation pipeline using a hierarchical intermediate representation (ChannelIR) that supports Kraus form and Pauli-sum structures, combined with algebraic rewriting and structure-aware circuit synthesis (LCU and channel-LCU).

**Model / system.** The framework models open quantum systems using Lindbladian master equations, representing continuous-time dynamics through Kraus operator-sum representations and short-time channel approximations.

**Key observables.** Gate count (CX, T, etc.), circuit depth, qubit count (width), and trace distance error.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Markovian dynamics, Lindbladian generators, and simulation error tolerances.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The dynamics are assumed to be Markovian, and the framework focuses on the high cost of non-Clifford (T) gates in fault-tolerant computing.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the ChannelIR Kraus form, circuit optimization for block-encoding, the complete compilation workflow, the syntax of the IR, and performance comparisons of gate counts and simulation errors against theoretical bounds.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of abstraction mismatch, presents the ChannelIR framework and its algebraic rewrite rules, details the LindFront frontend and circuit synthesis backend, provides formal proofs for rule correctness and optimization complexity, and concludes with benchmarks on Lindbladian simulation and scalability.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Most quantum compilers assume programs are reversible unitary circuits. This fits closed-system algorithms, but not open-system simulation, where the natural program objects are quantum channels describing non-unitary dynamics. We present a channel-first compilation framework that treats channels as first-class compilation objects. Our core IR, ChannelIR, represents channels explicitly in Kraus form, a standard channel representation, with Pauli-sum structure, enabling algebraic rewrites before circuit synthesis. We instantiate the framework with LindFront, a frontend that lowers continuous-time Lindbladian generators to short-time channels, and a backend that compiles these channels to executable circuits with structure-aware optimizations. On Lindbladian and channel-simulation benchmarks, the optimized pipeline reduces gate count by up to 99% over an unoptimized channel-first baseline and scales better than circuit-first Stinespring compilation.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.23514"></a>
### [Minimal Trade-off and Optimal Measurement for Multiparameter Quantum Estimation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23514v1)

**Authors:** Lingna Wang, Hongzhen Chen, Haidong Yuan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23514v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23514_figures/2605.23514_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Geometrical picture of the measurement incom- patibility. In the measurement basis {|m⟩}, {|oj⟩} are all real vectors while the vectors {|lj⟩} are in general complex. The incompatibility-induced trade-off is quantified by the Eu- clidean distances P</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.23514_figures/2605.23514_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Radar sensing of target’s range and velocity using entangled biphoton state.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.23514_figures/2605.23514_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Uncertainty relations in quantum radar using bipho- ton states with different amounts of entanglement (κ). Here the dashed lines denote the uncertainty relations obtained from QCRB and the solid lines denote the refined Arthurs- Kelly uncertainty relations, which is achievable for the simul- taneous estimation of range and velocity. The shaded areas illustrate the gap between the QCRB and the tight bound.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a new analytical bound that quantifies the precision loss in multiparameter quantum estimation caused by measurement incompatibility. It also introduces a systematic way to design optimal measurements that reach these limits, demonstrated through a refined uncertainty relation for quantum radar.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental tool for quantum metrology and sensing, specifically addressing how to optimize simultaneous measurements in the presence of non-commuting observables, which is critical for quantum-enhanced sensing technologies.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The fundamental difficulty in simultaneous multiparameter quantum estimation due to the incompatibility of optimal measurements, which creates a precision gap between achievable and ultimate quantum limits.

**Main result.** The derivation of a new tight analytical bound for the precision trade-off in pure state estimation and a systematic methodology for designing optimal measurements that saturate this bound.

**Method.** A geometric approach using the complex geometric tensor (combining QFI and Berry curvature) and an optimization strategy that models POVMs as projective measurements on an augmented system-ancilla state.

**Model / system.** The framework is applied to pure quantum states and specifically demonstrated using a quantum radar model involving entangled biphotons used for simultaneous range and velocity estimation.

**Key observables.** Quantum Fisher Information matrix (F_Q), imaginary part of the geometric tensor (F_Im), and the precision metric Gamma.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of parameters (n), entanglement parameter (kappa), and the eigenvalues (lambda_q) of the normalized incompatibility matrix.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary focus is on pure quantum states and the assumption that parameters are locally identifiable (invertible QFI).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a geometric visualization of measurement incompatibility; Figure 2 shows a schematic of a quantum radar setup; Figure 3 plots uncertainty relations for different entanglement levels.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of measurement incompatibility, presents a new analytical bound for precision trade-offs, provides a constructive method for optimal measurement design, and validates the theory through a quantum radar application.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A fundamental challenge in multiparameter quantum estimation arises from the incompatibility of optimal measurements for different parameters, leading to intricate precision trade-offs that obscure the understanding of ultimate quantum limits. Here, we present an approach that precisely quantifies these trade-offs for an arbitrary number of parameters encoded in pure quantum states. Our approach not only derives tight analytical bounds for the trade-offs induced by measurement incompatibility but also provides a systematic methodology to design optimal measurement strategies that saturate these limits. To demonstrate the practical significance of our findings, we apply our framework to quantum radar and obtain a refined Arthurs-Kelly relation that characterizes the ultimate performance for the simultaneous estimation of range and velocity with any given amount of entanglement. This showcases the transformative potential of our findings for a wide range of applications in quantum metrology, sensing, and beyond.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.23670"></a>
### [Twirled Perfect Tensor Networks: Computationally covariant holographic tensor networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23670v1)

**Authors:** Gurbir Arora, Matthew Headrick, Albion Lawrence, Martin Sasieta, Brian Swingle, Connor Wolfe  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23670v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23670_figures/2605.23670_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: A semiclassical python’s lunch in EWA (gray).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.23670_figures/2605.23670_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Left: TN model of a python’s lunch, with three cuts associated with the minimal QES XA, bulge Xb A and constriction Xc A, whose Hilbert spaces are mapped by the two isometries V1, V2. Right: The map V = V1V † 2 viewed as a unitary U together with postselection onto the state |0⟩⊗m of the m qubits (in red). The amount of postselected and ancilla qubits is m = Sgen(Xb A) −Sgen(Xc A) and n = Sgen(Xb A) −Sgen(XA), respectively. Reconstructing the lunch amounts to finding an approximate unitary U0 that reproduces the measurement, which is famously hard.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.23670_figures/2605.23670_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: A local TN model of the python’s lunch. In gray, a foliation γ of the TN with the associated max cut Xγ in red.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.23670_figures/2605.23670_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: A planar python’s lunch (in blue) delimited in the transverse direction by the spatial IR regulator. As the IR cutoff LIR is increased, the bulge undergoes a transition from the naive symmetric bulge to an extremal surface which spontaneously breaks the Z2 spatial reflection symmetry of the IR regulator. In the thermodynamic limit LIR →∞, the bulge hugs the constriction and they only differ at a finite distance from the cutoff. This produces a PLC exponent which is non-extensive in the system’s spatial volume. (Figure adapted from [48].)</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.23670_figures/2605.23670_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: TN model of a planar python’s lunch. Two MERA TFDs are contracted together at some radial cutoff by a chain of local unitaries (red boxes) associated with the thin matter domain wall. The map V defined by this TN admits a simple factorization V = V1V † 2 along the dashed cut. The grey lines denote a standard planar-symmetric foliation and associated contraction scheme. Because the number of postselections required is proportional to LIR, this way of contracting the network yields a complexity log CTN ∝LIR. This is parametrically larger than the PLC prediction.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper identifies that standard random tensor networks fail to accurately model the complexity predicted by the Python's Lunch Conjecture due to a lack of computational covariance. To fix this, the authors introduce 'twirled perfect tensor networks' which successfully recover the predicted complexity bounds and maintain holographic properties. This provides a more robust framework for studying the computational complexity of black hole interiors.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the discrepancy between the Python's Lunch Complexity (PLC) prediction and the complexity of generic tensor network models, specifically addressing the lack of computational covariance in standard random tensor networks.

**Main result.** The authors propose a new class of 'twirled perfect tensor networks' that satisfy computational covariance, thereby aligning the complexity of the network with the PLC prediction and obeying a lattice Ryu-Takayanagi formula.

**Method.** The study uses geometric analysis (min-max theory, Mean Curvature Flow), random matrix theory (Wishart matrix analysis, Marchenko-Pastur law), and complexity quantification via circuit complexity and isometry decomposition.

**Model / system.** The paper focuses on holographic tensor network models (including Random Tensor Networks, MERA, and MPS) representing the 'Python's Lunch' geometry in AdS/CFT, characterized by a bulge and a constriction in the entanglement wedge.

**Key observables.** Python's Lunch Complexity (PLC), entanglement entropy (von Neumann and generalized), circuit complexity, and the Morse index of extremal surfaces.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Large N limit, large bond dimension limit, Markov length, and the ratio of dimension between input and output ancilla qubits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis primarily focuses on time-symmetric settings and the regime where the area term dominates the generalized entropy; it also assumes a compact and connected manifold for geometric proofs.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the Python's Lunch geometry with minimal, bulge, and outermost surfaces; Figure 2 illustrates the tensor network model and the map as a postselected unitary; Figure 3 shows the foliation and cuts; Figure 18 compares different length scales.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by identifying the failure of random tensor networks to match PLC predictions, introduces the concept of computational covariance, proposes the twirled perfect tensor network model, proves its properties using geometric and statistical tools, and concludes with discussions on limitations and implications for holography.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We define a novel class of tensor networks motivated by the Python's Lunch Conjecture (PLC) in local tensor network models of the black hole interior. We start from the observation that, for extended black brane states with short-range correlations, the PLC predicts a complexity that is smaller than the upper bound for generic short-range correlated states. We argue that the PLC makes implicit assumptions about the fine structure of the relevant tensor networks modeling gravity that render them non-generic. We demonstrate this explicitly in random tensor network models of the python's lunch, where the exponential complexity is not generally controlled by the PLC exponent. We trace the difference with the PLC to a lack of "computational covariance" in random tensor networks: while the PLC is motivated by an ability to arbitrarily decompose space into low-complexity units provided certain basic rules are followed, we show that random tensor networks do not generically have this property. We propose another class of tensor networks built from what we call "twirled perfect tensors" that do satisfy the computational covariance property and have a complexity bounded by the PLC value. We still find a discrete limitation from local postselection that appears to be absent in gravity. Moreover, we show that this class of tensor networks combines desirable holographic features of perfect tensor networks and random tensor networks, for example, it obeys a lattice Ryu-Takayanagi formula for arbitrary boundary subregions. Though motivated by holography, these tensor networks provide a flexible framework with potential applications beyond quantum gravity.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.23755"></a>
### [Kondo singlet from ferromagnetic coupling: an analog of Anderson-Morel superconductivity in the magnetic channel](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23755v1)

**Authors:** Ewan Scott, Yaqi Chen, Michael Turaev, Tarkan Yzeiri, Chris Hooley, Krzysztof P. Wójcik, Michał P. Kwasigroch  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23755v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **4/5**

**Summary.** This paper shows that purely ferromagnetic exchange can lead to a Kondo singlet ground state if the coupling is energy-dependent. This mechanism is an analog of Anderson-Morel superconductivity and can lead to a heavy Fermi liquid state that is preferred over magnetic ordering.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether a Kondo spin-singlet ground state can emerge from purely ferromagnetic (FM) spin-exchange coupling between a magnetic impurity and a conduction electron sea.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that an energy-dependent FM coupling can renormalize to an antiferromagnetic (AFM) coupling at low temperatures, leading to a Kondo singlet and a heavy Fermi liquid state in a lattice, effectively creating an 'inverted' Doniach phase diagram.

**Method.** The study employs perturbative Renormalization Group (RG) to analyze coupling flow and a specialized two-mode Numerical Renormalization Group (NRG) to study the strong-coupling regime.

**Model / system.** A Kondo model featuring a magnetic impurity coupled to a conduction sea via a non-separable, energy-dependent, and fully SU(2)-symmetric ferromagnetic exchange interaction $J_{kq}$. The model also considers a Kondo lattice of such impurities.

**Key observables.** Impurity entropy contribution, fixed-point spectrum, hybridization order parameter, and Kondo temperature ($T_K$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Exchange couplings $J_1$ and $J_2$, energy window width $W$, bandwidth $D$, and Fermi energy $\epsilon_F$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The interaction strength is reduced within a specific energy window $W$ around the Fermi level; the conduction band is treated as two distinct channels (one gapless, one gapped).

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows electron scattering and the structure of $J_{kq}$ and impurity entropy; Fig 2 shows RG trajectories; Fig 3 illustrates the hybridization fields and real-space hybridization dip; Fig 4 shows the modified Wilson chain; Fig 5 plots the Kondo temperature scaling.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the FM coupling problem, presents the energy-dependent Hamiltonian, details the RG and 2-mode NRG methodology, analyzes the single-impurity singlet formation, extends the results to the Kondo lattice (heavy Fermi liquid), and concludes with experimental realization routes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider magnetic impurities coupled to a conduction sea via a fully isotropic ferromagnetic spin-exchange term, the strength of which depends on the conduction-electron modes involved in the scattering. In the single-impurity case we show both analytically and numerically that there exists a parameter regime in which the conventional Kondo effect develops at low temperatures, leading to a singlet ground state. In the case of a lattice of impurities, we show that this leads to a heavy Fermi liquid state that is energetically favored over magnetic ordering in a broad parameter range. We argue that these effects are analogs of Anderson-Morel superconductivity, and discuss routes to their experimental realization.

</details>

### [The negativity core of a 1+1D massless scalar quantum field](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23824v1)

**Authors:** Jason Pye, Atharva Hingane, Robert H. Jonsson  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23824v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23824_figures/2605.23824_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Smearing functions defining the negativity cores for the three modes with the largest contributions to the negativity for A = (−2, −1) and B = (0.5, 3).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.23824_figures/2605.23824_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Numerical values of the contribution to logarithmic negativity of each eigenvalue of J</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides an analytical solution for the entanglement between two disjoint intervals in a 1+1D massless scalar field. It identifies 'negativity cores,' which are the specific localized modes that constitute the entanglement. The results bridge the gap between local field theory and the geometric structure of quantum correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides fundamental insights into the spatial structure of vacuum entanglement and the identification of specific field modes that carry correlations, which is highly relevant for understanding entanglement in continuous variable systems and quantum field theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to provide a complete characterization of the entanglement structure between two bounded, spacelike-separated regions in a (1+1)-dimensional free massless real scalar field, specifically identifying the localized modes that carry the entanglement.

**Main result.** The authors derive closed-form analytical solutions for the logarithmic negativity and identify the 'negativity cores,' which are the specific localized field modes responsible for the entanglement.

**Method.** The study employs Gaussian state methods and a framework based on the Kähler structure of Gaussian states, reducing the problem to a boundary value problem in the complex plane involving the diagonalization of an integral operator.

**Model / system.** A (1+1)-dimensional free massless real scalar quantum field in its vacuum state, where the system consists of two disjoint, bounded intervals on the real axis.

**Key observables.** Logarithmic negativity (En) and negativity cores (localized smearing functions/modes).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The cross ratio (eta) characterizing the geometry, the ratio of separation to interval length (d/l), and the large separation (eta -> 0) and small separation (eta -> 1) regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Observers are assumed to couple only to the derivatives of the field rather than the field amplitude, and the field is assumed to be in its vacuum state.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the spatial localization of the negativity cores by displaying the real part of the smearing functions for the first three modes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of vacuum entanglement, defines the (1+1)D scalar field model, presents the analytical derivation of the negativity and its mode decomposition using Gaussian state methods, validates the results with a lattice-based numerical model, and discusses asymptotic scaling regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Vacuum entanglement is a fundamental feature of quantum field theory exhibiting rich structure that is not completely understood. Here, we provide a complete characterization of the entanglement between two bounded spacelike-separated regions in a (1+1)-dimensional free massless real scalar field. Employing Gaussian state methods, we analytically compute the logarithmic negativity and construct closed-form solutions for the localized modes carrying it, called negativity cores. These results deepen our understanding of quantum fields and suggest extensions to higher dimensions and fermionic fields.

</details>

### [Multi-flux Aharonov-Bohm caging with tunable couplings](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23546v1)

**Authors:** Le-Chuan Wang, Sai Li, Jia Liu, Zheng-Yuan Xue

**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper proposes a method to achieve Aharonov-Bohm caging in lattices with multiple flux parameters, allowing for highly tunable wavefunction localization. It demonstrates that while this caging is a powerful tool for quantum simulation, it is sensitive to environmental perturbations like noise and dissipation. The findings are applicable to various platforms including photonics, superconducting qubits, and trapped ions.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a scalable framework for engineering flat-band localization and explores the 'inverse Anderson transition' where disorder enables transport, which is highly relevant to open quantum systems and many-body dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the universal conditions for Aharonov-Bohm (AB) caging in multi-flux lattices and exploring how this localization effect is destroyed by disorder, dissipation, and non-Hermitian effects.

**Main result.** The authors derive universal conditions for AB caging in multi-flux lattices and demonstrate that while the effect is robust to certain configurations, it is cumulatively destroyed by increasing on-site detuning, dissipation, or non-Hermitian hopping.

**Method.** The study uses theoretical derivation of eigenvalue equations in k-space combined with numerical simulations of photon dynamics and Lindblad master equations to analyze localization and transport.

**Model / system.** A 1D multi-flux lattice with tunable couplings and gauge fields, applicable to photonic lattices, superconducting circuits (circuit QED), and trapped-ion systems.

**Key observables.** Inverse Participation Number (IPN), IPN fluctuation (sigma), and group velocity (vg).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Transition phases (phi), coupling strength (J), disorder strength (Delta), dissipation rate (gamma), and non-Hermitian hopping strength (Gamma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The physical realizations assume the validity of the rotating-wave approximation (RWA).

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the lattice structure; Fig 2 displays numerical simulations of photon localization and transport; Fig 5 shows IPN time evolution under disorder; Fig 6 and 7 present phase diagrams and statistical localization criteria.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the AB caging concept, proposes a scalable protocol for multi-flux caging, validates it via numerical simulations, investigates the impact of disorder and dissipation, and discusses experimental implementations in quantum platforms.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Aharonov-Bohm (AB) caging is the complete wavefunction localization effect in translational-invariant lattices induced by destructive phase interference. These phases originate from the gauge fields such as the penetrated magnetic fields, which are directly related to several novel topological quantum states of matter. Recently, this effect has demonstrated significant potential for applications in quantum simulation and topological quantum computation. Here, we propose a scalable protocol to derive universal conditions for AB caging with multi-flux. The numerical simulations validate the theoretical predictions by directly observing AB caging phenomena. We also investigate the breakage of the caging effect with onsite detuning. Our protocol can be directly tested in several quantum many-body platforms and provides an alternative approach for advancing quantum simulation of exotic state matter.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.23639"></a>
### [Quantum Ghost Spectroscopy Reveals Hidden Electronic Coherence in Molecular Aggregates](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23639v1)

**Authors:** Mingran Zhang, Yihe Xu, Vladislav V. Yakovlev, Zhedong Zhang

**Summary.** This paper introduces a quantum ghost spectroscopy technique using entangled photons to overcome the Fourier uncertainty limit in ultrafast spectroscopy. By decoupling temporal and spectral resolutions, the authors reveal hidden electronic-to-vibrational coherence transfer in molecular aggregates. This approach provides a new way to observe high-frequency quantum dynamics that are otherwise obscured in classical measurements.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates a quantum-enhanced spectroscopic technique that bypasses fundamental classical limits (Fourier uncertainty) to resolve ultrafast nonadiabatic dynamics, which is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Conventional ultrafast spectroscopy is limited by the Fourier uncertainty principle, creating a trade-off between temporal and spectral resolution that obscures hidden electronic and vibrational coherence dynamics.

**Main result.** Time-resolved quantum ghost spectroscopy (tr-QGS) using entangled photons enables independent control of time and energy scales, successfully revealing previously hidden 0.7 eV electronic coherence and the subsequent transfer to vibrational coherence.

**Method.** The study combines a theoretical framework for tr-QGS using fourth-order perturbative expansion with Time-Dependent Density Matrix Renormalization Group (TD-DMRG) simulations.

**Model / system.** The system consists of perylene bismide (PBI-1) trimers in a dilute solvent, modeled with a Hamiltonian including electronic states (S1, S2), five vibrational modes, and nonadiabatic vibronic coupling.

**Key observables.** Electronic coherence (rho_S1,S2), population dynamics (S2 to S1 internal conversion), and vibrational coherence (mode position dynamics).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Electronic energy difference of ~0.7 eV, temporal scales of 5-50 fs for electronic and 200-300 fs for vibrational coherence, and sub-shot-noise sensitivity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies on the validity of fourth-order perturbative expansion and assumes the initial state is created by the signal photon pulse.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the molecular structure, potential energy surfaces, and the tr-QGS experimental setup; Figure 2 shows coherence dynamics; Figure 6 illustrates the comparison between time- and frequency-gated signals.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Fourier limitation problem, proposes the tr-QGS platform, details the TD-DMRG simulation methodology, presents results on population and coherence dynamics, and discusses broader applications.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Ultrafast spectroscopy of molecular systems is fundamentally constrained by the Fourier uncertainty principle: high temporal resolution smears out electronic state signatures, while high spectral resolution obscures dynamic information. Here we overcome this limitation using time-resolved quantum ghost spectroscopy (tr-QGS) with entangled photon pairs, which enables independent control of temporal and spectral scales. We apply this approach to perylene bismide (PBI-1) trimers for energy transfer,by combining a quantum description of light-molecule interaction with time-dependent density matrix renormalization group (TD-DMRG) simulations. This explicitly includes five vibrational modes and nonadiabatic coupling between electronic states. Our simulations reveal that tr-QGS uniquely captures electronic coherence oscillating at 0.7 eV for >50 fs, a signature of nonadiabatic coupling that was obscured in conventional time-resolved fluorescence due to Fourier-limited broadening. Moreover, we observe a direct transfer from electronic to vibrational coherence at 200 fs, providing real-time visualization of vibronic relaxation pathways. The entangled photon correlation enables a sensitivity below the shot-noise limit and suppresses photobleaching artifacts that plague classical measurements. These results establish tr-QGS as a transformative tool for interrogating nonadiabatic dynamics in molecular aggregates, light-harvesting complexes, and photocatalysts, offering a route to reveal quantum coherence in chemistry with unprecedented time-energy precision.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.23803"></a>
