<a id="paper-2605.22980"></a>
### [Automatic De-Quantization of Quantum Programs Using Constant Propagation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22980v1)

**Authors:** Lian Remme, Alexander Weinert, Andre Waschk, Lukas Burgholzer, Robert Wille  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22980v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.22980_figures/2605.22980_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: An example for a dynamic circuit. The left and right circuits are equivalent. The quantum control is removed in exchange for a classical condition on a gate.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.22980_figures/2605.22980_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: Measurement lifting in action: By commuting the measurement from Figure 2a forward to create the circuit in Figure 2b, a quantum control can be replaced with a classical control. This creates the circuit in Figure 2c and eliminates a costly two-qubit gate. All three circuits are equivalent.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.22980_figures/2605.22980_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3: Hadamard lifting: By commuting Pauli and Hadamard gates, measurements can be lifted further, enabling more quantum resource reduction. To create the circuit in Figure 3b from Figure 3a, the Hadamard gate is lifted in front of the Pauli gate. Afterwards, the measurement is lifted in front of the Pauli gate and the result negated, to create Figure 3c. All three circuits are equivalent.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.22980_figures/2605.22980_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 6: If we have a CNOT target followed by a Hadamard gate followed by a measurement, one can change this to a measurement following the control. First, the target and control is flipped by applying four new Hadamard gates, which changes the circuit from the first to the second one. Then, the two Hadamard gates after each other cancel out, yielding the third circuit.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.22980_figures/2605.22980_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 8: The union table that results from the circuit in Figure 7. Adapted from [4, Figure 3].</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a compiler optimization technique called hybrid quantum-classical constant propagation that identifies and replaces unnecessary quantum gates with classical instructions. By tracking both quantum and classical information, the method reduces the resource burden on error-prone NISQ hardware. The approach is implemented in the MQT Core toolkit and shows significant promise for reducing multi-qubit gate counts in complex algorithms.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need to optimize NISQ-era quantum circuits by reducing costly quantum operations and replacing them with more efficient classical computations.

**Main result.** The proposed hybrid quantum-classical constant propagation technique effectively reduces quantum gate counts and controlled gates, particularly when combined with measurement lifting, though it increases compilation time.

**Method.** A static analysis technique using a formal hybrid state model to track both quantum amplitudes and classical registers, implemented via the MQT Core compiler using MLIR.

**Model / system.** Dynamic quantum-classical circuits consisting of unitary gates, measurements, and resets, specifically targeting NISQ platforms like superconducting and trapped-ion QPUs.

**Key observables.** Quantum gate count, controlled gate count, and compilation duration.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Error rates of superconducting and trapped-ion hardware, and the N,M-representability limits used for abstract execution.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The optimization assumes the ability to implement dynamic circuits with classical-quantum feedback loops and relies on an artificial upper bound for state space complexity during analysis.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate circuit transformations for measurement and Hadamard lifting, the use of hybrid union tables for state tracking, and bar charts comparing resource reduction and compilation time across various algorithms.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the resource optimization problem, formalizes a hybrid quantum-classical machine state model, introduces specific optimization routines (constant propagation, measurement/Hadamard lifting), describes the MQT Core implementation, and evaluates performance using MQT Bench.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum computing promises to solve problems beyond the reach of classical computers, but today's quantum hardware is error-prone and much slower than classical hardware. Every quantum operation is costly, making it crucial to minimize quantum resource usage in near-term algorithms. Quantum resources should only be used when they are truly essential for quantum advantage, and not wasted on operations that can be efficiently handled by classical computation.   In this work, we focus on de-quantizing quantum operations to classical computation whenever possible. The approach we propose for this is hybrid quantum-classical constant propagation, an optimization which reduces quantum operations by trading them for fast, reliable classical instructions. This is done by tracking between quantum and classical states to identify and eliminate unnecessary quantum gates and controls.   We formalize a hybrid state model for quantum-classical constant propagation, implement our optimizations in the open-source MQT Core tool, and evaluate them on benchmark circuits.   The obtained results show that quantum-classical constant propagation can reduce costly multi-qubit operations, making quantum programs more practical and robust for near-term devices. This opens the door to new hybrid compiler strategies that leverage the best of both quantum and classical worlds.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.23792"></a>
### [Complementing Quantum Error Correction in Quantum Metrology via Swap Test](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23792v1)

**Authors:** Xiaodie Lin, Linxuan Li, Haidong Yuan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23792v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

</details>

<sub>Fig. 5: Single-parameter estimation errors for different methods. The absolute parameter estimation errors, |ˆλ −λ|, are shown for the original noisy (blue), VSP-based (orange), and our (green) methods. <details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.23792_figures/2605.23792_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 6: Multi-parameter estimation errors for different methods. Panel (a) shows results under dephasing noise, while (b) shows results under IIDP noise. </details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a swap test-based method to complement quantum error correction in quantum metrology. It specifically targets 'indistinguishable noise' that standard QEC cannot fix, providing a way to achieve Heisenberg-limited precision even under high noise levels. The proposed method outperforms existing virtual state purification techniques in both single- and multi-parameter estimation tasks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and quantum optics as it provides a practical strategy to extend the lifetime of quantum-enhanced sensing in the presence of non-correctable, indistinguishable noise.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Quantum metrology precision is limited by noise that is indistinguishable from the signal, which standard Quantum Error Correction (QEC) cannot address. Additionally, existing mitigation techniques like Virtual State Purification (VSP) suffer from significant noise accumulation over time.

**Main result.** The authors propose a swap test-based method that achieves Heisenberg scaling in precision and remains robust against noise accumulation. The method provides an unbiased estimator for parameters even under high levels of IID Pauli noise.

**Method.** A swap test-based protocol is used to estimate error rates directly, allowing for the construction of an analytical unbiased estimator for the target parameter. The method utilizes two copies of the noisy state to determine state overlap.

**Model / system.** The system consists of n-qubit GHZ-like probe states undergoing evolution under a Hamiltonian (single-parameter: H = lambda * sum(Z); multi-parameter: H = lambda1 * X...X + lambda2 * sum(Z)). The noise models include local dephasing and general Independent and Identically Distributed Pauli (IIDP) noise.

**Key observables.** The Pauli X operator expectation value from the swap test, the noisy expectation value of a target observable A, and the error rate p_odd.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Target parameter lambda, number of qubits n, evolution time t, local error rates p_z and p_x, and the Heisenberg and Standard Quantum Limits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the use of periodic QEC to reduce IIDP noise to effectively dephasing noise and uses first-order Taylor expansions for error scaling approximations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the general quantum metrology scheme and state preparation; Figure 4 shows error scaling (bias and variance) under IIDP noise as functions of lambda, p_1, and n.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of indistinguishable noise, proposes a swap test-based mitigation method, derives an analytical unbiased estimator, performs error scaling analysis (variance and bias), and validates the method through numerical simulations for single- and multi-parameter estimation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The precision and sensitivity achievable in quantum metrology are often compromised by the presence of noise. While quantum error correction has emerged as a promising strategy, it is ineffective in addressing noise that is indistinguishable from the signal. To address this challenge, virtual state purification was introduced as a complementary approach to quantum error correction. However, significant noise accumulation can impede its performance. To overcome this limitation, we propose a swap test-based method specifically designed to address indistinguishable noise, even under high noise levels. A systematic error-scaling analysis demonstrates that this method enables quantum-enhanced precision in certain scenarios. Furthermore, numerical simulations demonstrate that our method surpasses virtual state purification in both single- and multi-parameter estimation tasks. The significant improvements in precision across diverse settings underscore the robustness and practicality of our method for real-world applications.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.23119"></a>
### [Construction of EAQECCs with imperfect ebits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23119v1)

**Authors:** Guanmin Guo, Ruihu Li  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23119v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23119_figures/2605.23119_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1 Comparative analysis of the Channel fidelity under four scenarios. We use the depolarizing rate pa and the Channel fidelity as the X and Y axes, respectively.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper extends the theory of entanglement-assisted quantum error correction to higher-dimensional qudit systems and incorporates the realistic scenario of noisy shared entanglement. It proves that if the shared entanglement is well-protected, these new codes can provide better error protection than standard stabilizer codes.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Generalizing the stabilizer formalism for Entanglement-Assisted Quantum Error-Correcting Codes (EAQECCs) from the binary case to the q-ary case while accounting for noise in the shared ebits.

**Main result.** The authors established a unified framework for constructing q-ary EAQECCs with noisy ebits (EAQECCs-Ne) and demonstrated that these codes can outperform standard stabilizer codes when ebit noise is sufficiently low.

**Method.** The study utilizes symplectic geometry over finite fields, the generalized Pauli group, and additive codes over quadratic extension fields to derive equivalent formulations and explicit code constructions.

**Model / system.** A quantum communication model involving a sender (Alice) and a receiver (Bob) using q-ary qudit systems, where both the transmitted qudits and the shared entanglement (ebits) are subject to depolarizing noise.

**Key observables.** Channel fidelity (approximate channel fidelity P(C))

**Important parameters / regimes.** q-ary dimension (q), code parameters [n, k, d; c], depolarizing rates for qudits (pa) and ebits (pb), and the entanglement degradation coefficient (lambda = pb/pa).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the existence of pre-existing entanglement between parties and relies on the mathematical properties of finite fields where q is a prime power.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a comparative analysis of channel fidelity versus the depolarizing rate pa under four different scenarios of ebit noise levels (ranging from pb = 0.01pa to pb = 0.99pa).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of noisy ebits, develops a generalized stabilizer formalism using symplectic geometry and additive codes, provides explicit construction methods for new q-ary EAQECCs-Ne, and concludes with a performance comparison against standard stabilizer codes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We generalize the stabilizer formalism for entanglement-assisted quantum error-correcting codes with noisy ebits (EAQECCs-Ne) from the binary case to the general $q$-ary case, where $q$ is a prime power. By leveraging the structure of the generalized Pauli group over $\mathbb{F}_q$ and symplectic geometry over $\mathbb{F}_q^{2n}$, we establish a unified framework for constructing EAQECCs-Ne for qudit systems. Equivalent formulations in terms of symplectic geometry over $\mathbb{F}_q$ and additive codes over $\mathbb{F}_q^{2n}$ are derived. We further construct several families of $q$-ary EAQECCs with noise ebits and analyze their performance compared to optimal stabilizer codes. Our results demonstrate that under certain noise conditions, the proposed EAQECCs-Ne can outperform standard stabilizer codes with equivalent error-correcting capability, offering a promising approach for fault-tolerant quantum computation in high-dimensional quantum systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.23324"></a>
### [Enhancing Blood Cells Classification using Hybrid Quantum Neural Networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23324v1)

**Authors:** Guilherme Cruz, Nouhaila Innan, Alberto Marchisio, Gabriel Falcao, Muhammad Shafique  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23324v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `QC/QI experiment` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.23324_figures/2605.23324_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. Overview of the proposed blood cell classification pipeline. The framework processes both the Blood Cell Images and PBC datasets through a common preprocessing and augmentation stage, followed by feature extraction using a pre-trained ResNet-50 backbone. The resulting 2048-dimensional feature vector is reduced to a 10-dimensional latent representation and passed to an HQNN composed of angle encoding, a variational quantum circuit with 10 qubits and 4 layers, and Pauli-Z measurement. The resulting quantum feature vector is then fed to a lightweight classification head for final blood cell type prediction.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.23324_figures/2605.23324_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5. Training and validation accuracy curves of the HQNN model on the Blood Cell Images Dataset.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that Hybrid Quantum Neural Networks can enhance the classification of complex medical images, such as blood cells, by providing superior feature representation. The researchers show that the quantum component improves the separability of visually similar cell types and remains robust even when executed on noisy IBM quantum hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of accurately classifying microscopic blood cells due to subtle morphological variations and high intra-class similarity in medical imaging.

**Main result.** Hybrid Quantum Neural Networks (HQNNs) outperform both classical baseline and parameter-matched classical models, specifically improving macro F1-scores by up to 3.7% in challenging classes.

**Method.** A hybrid pipeline using a pre-trained ResNet-50 backbone for feature extraction, followed by a dimensionality reduction layer and a variational quantum circuit (VQC) for quantum-enhanced feature transformation.

**Model / system.** A 10-qubit variational quantum circuit using angle encoding (RY rotations) and a ring-structured CNOT entangling pattern, implemented via PennyLane and tested on both simulators and IBM quantum hardware.

**Key observables.** Accuracy, macro F1-score, precision, recall, and multiclass ROC-AUC.

**Important parameters / regimes.** 10 qubits, 4 variational layers, 2048-dimensional feature vector reduced to a 10-dimensional latent bottleneck.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The performance gains are attributed to quantum feature transformations rather than simple increases in parameter count; the study assumes the utility of the NISQ-era hardware for such tasks despite noise.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows blood cell classes; Figure 2 is a pipeline flowchart; Figure 3 is a VQC schematic; Figures 4-5 show training convergence; Figures 6-12 present performance comparisons, confusion matrices, and per-class F1-scores.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the medical problem, describes the HQNN architecture and comparison framework, details the experimental setup on datasets and hardware, presents performance results across different models and data scales, and concludes with limitations and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Accurate classification of microscopic blood cells is still a critical task in medical image analysis, where subtle variations and limited data can challenge conventional deep learning models. As such, we investigate in this work the potential of Hybrid Quantum-Classical Neural Networks (HQNNs) to enhance feature representation and improve classification performance in this domain. We propose a modular architecture combining a pre-trained ResNet-50 backbone with a low-dimensional latent bottleneck and a variational quantum circuit, enabling a direct comparison between quantum-enhanced and purely classical transformation mechanisms. To isolate the contribution of the quantum component, we evaluate three architectures: a HQNN model, a Classical Matched Model with an additional nonlinear transformation layer of comparable capacity, and a baseline model without an intermediate transformation stage. Experiments conducted on two publicly available blood cell datasets, namely the Blood Cell Images dataset and the PBC dataset, demonstrate that HQNNs consistently achieve superior or more balanced performance across evaluation metrics. In the Blood Cell Images Dataset, the proposed approach improves macro F1-score by up to 3.7% compared to classical baselines, while improving the F1-score from 98.54% to 98.69% in the more challenging 8-class scenario with near-saturated performance. Additional evaluation on IBM quantum hardware shows that the model remains robust under noise, with only a modest performance degradation relative to simulated results. These results indicate that quantum feature transformations can enhance discriminative representations, particularly in challenging classification scenarios, and highlight the practical potential of HQNN models for medical imaging tasks.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.23431"></a>
### [Generalized Shift Vector as the Intrinsic Dipole of Many-Body Correlated Electronic States](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23431v1)

**Authors:** Jiaming Hu, Sudipta Kundu, Zhichao Guo, Joshua J. P. Thompson, Wenbin Li, Hua Wang, Bartomeu Monserrat  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23431v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23431_figures/2605.23431_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematics of the physical manifestations of the exciton shift vector. (a) Real-space shift of the (coarse-grained) probability density: the maximum peak is displaced from xi −xj = 0 (solid curve) to xi −xj = −XS,Q 1 (dashed curve). (b) Linear Stark effect of the exciton under a homogeneous electrostatic field F in a finite system. The state S = 1 (S = 2) possesses a finite (vanishing) shift vec- tor, and therefore exhibits (does not exhibit) a linear field response (red line) as the leading contribution at small |F |.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.23431_figures/2605.23431_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. First-principles calculation of monolayer MoS2. The internal phase of x-direction exciton dipole arg(AS,0 cvk rb1 cv,k) of (a) 7th (b) 8th and (c) 11th lowest-energy exciton states (unit: π; b1 aligns with one of the lattice vectors ( √</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper reinterprets the shift vector as an intrinsic dipole moment of a single many-body correlated state rather than a property of transitions between states. This unified framework encompasses various phenomena including excitonic and electron-phonon-mediated processes. It demonstrates that the shift vector captures essential phase information within many-body correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a unified geometric and dipolar interpretation of many-body correlations, connecting internal phase coherence to observable transport and optical phenomena like the shift current.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Redefining the shift vector, a key quantity in nonlinear optics and transport, from a transition-based concept between two states to a fundamental intrinsic dipole property of a single many-body correlated state.

**Main result.** The authors establish that the shift vector is the intrinsic dipole moment of a correlated state, providing a unified framework that recovers optical, excitonic, and electron-phonon shift vectors as special cases.

**Method.** A theoretical derivation using many-body wavefunctions expanded in a Bloch-product basis, employing a branch-fixed approach to ensure gauge invariance and analyzing the expectation value of the electric dipole operator.

**Model / system.** Many-body correlated electronic states, specifically including N-particle states such as excitons (electron-hole pairs), Cooper pairs, trions, and biexcitons in periodic systems.

**Key observables.** Generalized shift vector (X_S), real-space joint probability density, linear Stark effect, and DC shift current.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Weak-correlation limit, two-band approximation, small-patch/short-range regime, and the phase-gradient of the many-body profile function.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes smooth U(1) gauge transformations, neglects edge effects in extended systems, and utilizes a weak-hybridization/small-relative-momentum expansion.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows schematics of real-space probability density displacement and the linear Stark effect; Figure 2 presents first-principles results for MoS2 illustrating the internal phase structure of different exciton states.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the shift vector problem, provides a many-body derivation of the generalized shift vector, proves gauge invariance, demonstrates the recovery of standard limits (optical/excitonic), and applies the framework to specific examples like MoS2 and Cooper pairs.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Shift vectors play a central role in nonlinear optics and transport phenomena, where they are usually understood as charge-center shifts associated with transitions between quantum states. Here we show that the same geometric structure can be more fundamentally understood as the intrinsic dipole moment of a single correlated state. Our derivation clarifies the local and global aspects of gauge invariance, the origin of the phase-gradient term, and its connection to the internal coherence structure of many-body correlations. The single-state shift character appears both as a displacement of the real-space joint probability density and as a linear electric-field modification in energy space. Applying this framework to optically induced correlations, electron-phonon-mediated processes, and excitonic electron-hole states, we recover previously proposed shift vectors and the standard expression for the shift current as special cases. Our results establish a common physical foundation for shift vectors as intrinsic dipolar properties of correlated electronic states.

</details>

### [Indefinite probabilities in quantum spacetime: A deepening of unpredictability](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23862v1)

**Authors:** Vittorio D'Esposito, Giuseppe Fabiano, Domenico Frattulillo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23862v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23862_figures/2605.23862_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.23862_figures/2605.23862_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.23862_figures/2605.23862_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.23862_figures/2605.23862_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores the concept of 'indefinite probabilities' arising from a non-commutative spacetime geometry. It shows that in a quantum-deformed universe, the probabilities of measurement outcomes do not commute, leading to a fundamental uncertainty in determining the relative orientation of different observers.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how non-commutative spacetime, modeled via the SUq(2) quantum group, leads to 'indefinite probabilities' where measurement outcome probabilities are non-commuting operators.

**Main result.** The authors derive an uncertainty principle for probability operators and rotation matrix elements, demonstrating that observers in a quantum-deformed spacetime cannot sharply measure their relative orientation.

**Method.** The study employs the algebraic framework of Doubly Quantum Mechanics (DQM) and braiding formalism using the R-matrix to compute commutators of probability operators in a leading-order expansion of the deformation parameter.

**Model / system.** A theoretical model of two observers (Alice and Bob) using spin-1/2 systems and Stern-Gerlach apparatuses within a non-commutative spacetime geometry described by the SUq(2) quantum group.

**Key observables.** Probability operators for spin outcomes (P(up) and P(down)) and rotation matrix elements (Rij) relating observer reference frames.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The deformation parameter q, which is related to the Planck length and cosmological constant, and the limit q -> 1 which recovers standard physics.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the validity of the SUq(2) quantum group as an effective description of spacetime and relies on a leading-order expansion in (1-q).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of non-commutative spacetime, presents the SUq(2) and DQM mathematical framework, derives the non-commutativity of probability operators via braiding, and concludes with the physical implications for observer orientation and uncertainty.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Non-commutative spacetime and quantum groups have been argued to capture non-classical features of spacetime and its symmetries in the low-energy limit of quantum gravity. In this letter, we show that employing the $SU_q(2)$ quantum group to describe rotational symmetry for spin-$\frac{1}{2}$ systems and Stern-Gerlach apparatuses leads to the description of probabilities of outcomes of spin measurements in terms of non-commuting operators. As a result, we obtain an uncertainty principle between different probability operators, realizing a notion of indefinite probabilities. This is then reflected in the non-commutativity of the entries of the rotation matrix relating the reference frames of two observers, hence fundamentally preventing them from sharply measuring their relative orientation.

</details>

### [Multi-field Return Point Memory](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23781v1)

**Authors:** Nathaniel Croce, Hossein Salahshoor, D. Zeb Rocklin

The fields then vary independently over time until reconverging to produce final configuration IV, which is identical to state II. Notice that Heven and Hodd never exceed the extrema reached during state I, consistent with the no-passing rule, and thus states II and III are less than I. **Summary.** This paper extends the concept of return-point memory from single-field to multi-field driving in disordered systems. It shows that while the order of applying different fields can lead to non-commutative behavior, the system can still be precisely controlled to return to exact previous microstates. This provides new insights into how complex, multistable physical systems can be trained or programmed.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether return-point memory (RPM)—the ability of a system to return to a previous exact microstate—persists when a system is driven by multiple independent control fields rather than a single scalar field.

**Main result.** The authors prove that multi-field driving leads to return-point memory where bounded field excursions restore the system to a previous microstate, while also demonstrating that non-monotonic field trajectories introduce non-commutativity.

**Method.** The study uses numerical simulations of a zero-temperature Ising model and mathematical frameworks involving the 'no-passing' property and partial ordering of microstates.

**Model / system.** A multi-field Ising model (MFIM) which generalizes the zero-temperature random-field Ising model (RFIM), featuring ferromagnetic coupling and multiple time-dependent control fields applied to different subsets of sites (e.g., checkerboard patterns).

**Key observables.** Magnetization (specifically even and odd site magnetizations), microscopic spin configurations, and the degree of partial ordering between states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling constant J, disorder strength sigma, field trajectories (monotonic vs. non-monotonic), and the spatial pattern of field application.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is studied in the zero-temperature limit, meaning no thermal fluctuations are present and spins only flip to minimize energy.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows spin configurations and checkerboard field application; Figure 3 demonstrates the restoration of microstates in two-field and large-scale 10,000-field systems; Figure 4 illustrates spiral trajectories in field space and the resulting periodic returns to specific magnetization states.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of multi-field control, defines the multi-field Ising model Hamiltonian, analyzes monotonic vs. non-monotonic field paths, proves the existence of multi-field RPM, and explores complex spiral dynamics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Non-equilibrium systems display memory, a dependence not merely on their present environment but on previously applied fields. Multistable systems such as spin glasses, martensites and granular matter have exponentially many microstates consistent with an applied field, making their rich dynamics difficult to control. Control and order can be achieved through the concept of partial ordering, which we here generalize to systems subject to multiple control fields. We demonstrate, within the model system of the zero-temperature Ising model, that this leads to return-point memory, in which an applied sequence of fields restores the hysteretic system not only to a previous magnetization, but to a previous exact microstate. The multiplicity of fields grants more precise and complex control of the system, with different classes of operations displaying commutative and noncommutative behavior. This grants new insight into how physical systems can remember, learn, and be trained.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.23789"></a>
### [Orientable Surfactants on Thin Liquid Films: A Dynamic Density-Functional Theory Approach](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23789v1)

**Authors:** Toby Kay, Serafim Kalliadasis  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23789v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23789_figures/2605.23789_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Sketch of the profile geometry for a thin liquid film on a planar horizontal substrate.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a new theoretical framework for modeling thin liquid films containing polar surfactants by accounting for their molecular orientation. By using Dynamic Density-Functional Theory, the authors show that surfactant alignment leads to a new form of generalized surface tension. This provides a more physically accurate way to describe the complex hydrodynamic stability of surfactant-laden interfaces.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Traditional thin-film models treat surfactants as symmetric, point-like particles, neglecting their amphiphilic, polar head-tail structure and the resulting effects on interfacial dynamics.

**Main result.** The authors derive a set of coupled evolution equations for film height, surfactant concentration, and polarization, uncovering a novel generalized surface tension that depends on both concentration and surfactant orientation.

**Method.** The study uses Dynamic Density-Functional Theory (DDFT) combined with a long-wave (lubrication) approximation and a moment-based closure approach to derive macroscopic transport equations from microscopic statistical mechanics.

**Model / system.** A thin liquid film on a planar horizontal substrate with a free surface containing insoluble, orientable, polar uniaxial surfactant particles undergoing Brownian motion.

**Key observables.** Film height (h), surfactant concentration (c), polarization field (P), and the alignment tensor (Q).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Translational diffusion tensor (parallel and perpendicular components), rotational diffusion coefficient, surface tension, and anchoring strength.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a long-wave approximation, uses an adiabatic approximation for higher-order moments (slaving Q to P), and assumes surfactants are insoluble and confined to the interface.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a geometric sketch of the thin liquid film on a substrate with a defined coordinate system and the free surface height.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the physical system, moves to a microscopic DDFT derivation for uniaxial particles, applies a long-wave expansion and moment-based coarse-graining, derives the coupled evolution equations, and concludes by demonstrating thermodynamic consistency and reduction to known non-polar models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Thin liquid films are ubiquitous across many natural and engineering systems, including films which are laden with surface active molecules, i.e. surfactants. The presence of surfactants may have a destabilising effect on the film owing to their influence on surface tension, the so-called Marangoni effect, which in turn can induce flows in the film. Classical thin-film models for surfactant-laden films lead to paradigmatic gradient dynamics equations governing the film height and surfactant concentration and have been widely studied. However, in all these works, which are based on fluid dynamics or nonequilibrium thermodynamics, the shape of surfactants is neglected, and they have been treated as symmetric point-like particles. In general, this is a drastic oversimplification, as surfactants are amphiphilic with a polar head-tail structure. To account for this effect we use elements from the statistical mechanics of classical fluids, namely density-functional theory (DFT), and its dynamic extension (DDFT). Starting from DDFT and under the long-wave approximation, we derive the pertinent thin-film equations with the surfactants treated as polar uniaxial particles. These are equations which govern the film height, as well as the surfactant concentration and polarisation field. They preserve the gradient dynamics form by appropriately defining the free energy, which contains the usual interfacial contributions, as well as further contributions from the polarisation field. In doing so, we uncover a novel form of a generalised surface tension that is dependent on the surfactant polarisation as well as concentration, and show that it arises in a thermodynamically consistent way.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.22909"></a>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new nonlinear cross-entropy metric to verify quantum advantage in shallow-depth random circuits. It proves that this metric is resistant to classical spoofing and is highly sample-efficient even in the presence of depolarizing noise. The work provides exact analytical solutions for these benchmarks using many-body physics techniques.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing benchmarks like linear cross-entropy (LXEB) for random circuit sampling can be classically spoofed by noise, making it difficult to verify quantum advantage in shallow-depth circuits.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that nonlinear cross-entropy (log XEB) provides a sample-efficient benchmark that can distinguish noisy quantum computers from classical spoofers, and they develop a HOG-based classifier with logarithmic sample complexity.

**Method.** The study uses the replica trick and large-n mean-field techniques applied to Brownian circuit ensembles, supplemented by numerical simulations of discrete Haar-random unitary circuits.

**Model / system.** The model consists of n-qubit all-to-all Brownian quantum circuits with single-qubit depolarizing noise, which can be mapped to a quantum statistical mechanics problem with an effective many-body Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Nonlinear cross-entropy (log XEB), Heavy Output Generation (HOG) fingerprints, and the variance of the XEB score.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit depth (parameterized by a), system size (n), and noise rate (gamma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes all-to-all connectivity and utilizes the large-n limit and the Brownian (continuous-time) approximation of discrete circuits.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the required number of samples for XEB estimation vs system size; Figure 2 illustrates the mapping to a statistical mechanics problem; Figure 6 shows numerical estimation of expected XEB and variance for discrete circuits.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the benchmarking challenge, defines the Brownian circuit model and its mapping to an effective Hamiltonian, derives analytic expressions for the nonlinear XEB and its variance using the replica trick, validates these results with numerical simulations of discrete circuits, and proposes a new HOG-based classifier.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Random circuit sampling (RCS) remains one of the most competitive frameworks for demonstrating quantum advantage in near-term noisy intermediate-scale quantum (NISQ) hardware. Unfortunately, absent error-correction, existing benchmarks to characterize these experiments, like linear cross-entropy, have been classically spoofed due to noise. Because of this, there are interesting regimes, like shallow-depth random quantum circuits, where sampling is plausibly classically intractable, but no existing benchmark can distinguish between a noisy quantum computer and an adversarial classical spoofer. In this paper, we demonstrate that the nonlinear cross-entropy provides a sample-efficient benchmark for shallow-depth all-to-all random quantum circuits whose score cleanly separates noisy quantum computers from state-of-the-art classical spoofers, even in the presence of depolarizing noise. Further, we develop a binary classifier based on the notion of heavy output generation that features logarithmic sample complexity at short depth. Our evidence comes from exact analytic expressions for all-to-all Brownian circuit ensembles derived using replica tricks, and numerical simulations that corroborate these results for discrete Haar-random unitary circuits.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a design strategy called 'selective Fermi-level pinning' to create highly efficient molecular rectifiers. By using asymmetric anchoring and specific molecular structures, the researchers show how to force transport through unoccupied orbitals, leading to rectification ratios as high as 10^3. This provides a general principle for engineering functional single-molecule electronic components.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving high rectification ratios in single-molecule junctions by overcoming the intrinsic symmetry of resonant tunneling and complex interfacial energy-level alignment.

**Main result.** The proposal of a 'selective Fermi-level pinning' strategy that achieves giant rectification ratios exceeding 10^3 in cyclo[n]carbon-based junctions by breaking transport symmetry.

**Method.** Computational modeling using Non-Equilibrium Green's Function (NEGF) combined with Density Functional Theory (DFT) via the Quantum ATK framework.

**Model / system.** A donor-acceptor molecular junction featuring a non-coplanary long-chain donor (1,2-diphenylethane), a wide-bandgap acceptor (cyclo[n]carbon), and asymmetric anchoring groups (-SH and -CN) between gold electrodes.

**Key observables.** Rectification Ratio (RR), current-voltage (I-V) characteristics, transmission spectra, and energy-level alignment (HOMO/LUMO positions).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Bias window, HOMO-LUMO energy gap, and the electrostatic potential difference (EDP) across the junction.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The transport is primarily governed by the alignment of molecular frontier orbitals with the electrode Fermi levels under bias.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the schematic of the rectifier and the mechanism of asymmetric tunneling; Figure 2 presents the device configuration and I-V characteristics; Figure 3 details the electronic structure, including spatial orbital separation and transmission spectra; Figure 4 explores the effects of geometric changes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of symmetry in molecular junctions, proposes the selective Fermi-level pinning strategy, demonstrates the performance using cyclo[n]carbon systems, analyzes the impact of structural variations (donor length and ring size), and concludes with the robustness of the design.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Molecular rectifiers are key functional components of molecular-scale integrated circuits, yet achieving high rectification ratios remains a longstanding challenge due to the intrinsic symmetry of resonant tunneling and the complexity of interfacial energy-level alignment. Here, we propose a rectifier design strategy based on selective Fermi-level pinning that breaks transport symmetry via pinning interactions between molecular frontier orbitals and electrodes. This framework enforces tunneling transport to be predominantly governed by unoccupied molecular orbitals, while substantially suppressing contributions from occupied states, thereby establishing a simplified and highly controllable rectification mechanism. The resulting cyclo[n]carbon-based molecular junctions exhibit giant rectification ratios exceeding 103, while retaining exceptional structural robustness against variations in both donor chain length and carbon ring size. This work reveals the critical role of selective Fermi-level pinning in molecular junctions and provides a general design principle for engineering functional single-molecule electronic devices.

</details>

### [Transition-state lattice modes and the breakdown of adiabatic tunneling for hydrogen and deuterium in bcc Nb](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23212v1)

**Summary.** This paper proves that the standard adiabatic approximation fails for hydrogen and deuterium tunneling in Niobium, showing instead that the process is a nonadiabatic, collective interaction with the lattice. This finding is crucial for understanding how interstitial defects contribute to decoherence in superconducting quantum circuits.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper provides a rigorous microscopic foundation for understanding how defect-induced tunneling in solids contributes to decoherence in superconducting qubits, specifically through the modification of the quasiparticle density of states.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the validity of the adiabatic approximation (separation of light-particle and lattice dynamics) for hydrogen and deuterium tunneling in bcc Niobium.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that adiabatic separation is only valid for extremely light particles like muons, whereas H and D tunneling is a fundamentally nonadiabatic, collective process mediated by anharmonic lattice couplings.

**Method.** The authors use a 5D Lattice-Renostormalized Born-Oppenheimer (LRBO) framework, solving a 5D Hamiltonian numerically using inputs from Density Functional Theory (DFT) and cubic spline interpolation.

**Model / system.** The system consists of interstitial hydrogen (H) and deuterium (D) trapped by oxygen impurities in a body-centered-cubic (bcc) Niobium (Nb) lattice, modeled via a 5D subspace including three interstitial modes and two lattice modes (Q and T).

**Key observables.** Tunnel splitting (J), wavefunction separability metric (epsilon_psi), and lattice-mode probability densities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mass regimes (H, D, and mu+), energy asymmetry (delta), and elastic energy cost (Ec).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The accuracy of the tunnel splitting calculation is approximately 60-70% of experimental values; the derivation assumes the self-trapped H is stable at one site in a fixed lattice.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of the tunneling subspace and lattice order parameters; Figure 2 presents potential energy surface and wavefunction contour plots for mu+ and H; Figure 5 displays lattice-mode probability density contours.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of adiabaticity in Nb, describes the 5D LRBO model and DFT methods, presents results comparing H/D to mu+ mass limits, discusses the breakdown of the adiabatic approximation, and concludes with implications for qubit decoherence.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Interstitial hydrogen and deuterium in body-centered-cubic metals constitute archetypal quantum tunneling systems. Their relevance has been renewed by the connection between hydrogenic tunneling in Nb and defect-induced decoherence in superconducting qubits, motivating a predictive microscopic theory. Existing theoretical treatments invoke an adiabatic separation between the light interstitial and the host lattice, an assumption whose validity has not been rigorously established for hydrogenic species. Here, we show that the experimentally measured tunnel splittings of O-trapped H and D in bcc Nb are quantitatively reproduced only within a five-dimensional (5D) Lattice-Renormalized Born-Oppenheimer (LRBO) framework. This approach treats three interstitial modes and two judiciously selected lattice modes, which includes a transition-state mode, on equal quantum footing. By recasting nested Born-Oppenheimer hierarchies within this same formalism and benchmarking against modern \textit{ab initio} potential energy surfaces, we show that adiabatic separation of the light particle from lattice dynamics is satisfied only in the positive-muon ($μ^{+}$) mass limit. In contrast, tunneling for H and D is fundamentally a collective, nonadiabatic process mediated by anharmonic lattice couplings. Finally, we show that the breakdown of adiabaticity can be anticipated from simple energy estimates involving the ground-state light-particle energy evaluated at a small number of fixed lattice configurations, providing a practical criterion for assessing the validity of adiabatic tunneling theories in other systems.

</details>

### [Fractionalization, emergent SU($N$) symmetries, and fragmentation in layered quantum spin-orbital models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22908v1)

**Authors:** Pedro M. Cônsoli, Aayush Vijayvargia, Onur Erten  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.22908v1>

**Summary.** This paper explores how stacking quantum spin-orbital layers can lead to emergent SU(N) symmetries and complex phase diagrams. It identifies a phenomenon called magnetic fragmentation, where different degrees of freedom (spin and orbital) exhibit fundamentally different types of order. This provides a theoretical framework for understanding intertwined orders and fractionalized quantum critical points.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates fractionalization, emergent SU(N) symmetries, and the coexistence of unconventional symmetry-breaking orders in layered quantum spin-orbital models.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that these models can realize a variety of competing phases, including flavor-selective localization and 'magnetic fragmentation,' where the spin sector exhibits local order while the orbital sector remains a quantum liquid with nontrivial string order.

**Method.** The study employs a Majorana fermion parton construction to map the Hamiltonian to an N-component Fermi Hubbard model, followed by mean-field theory and finite-size scaling analysis.

**Model / system.** A family of layered quantum spin-orbital models consisting of N stacked square-lattice layers with Kitaev-type intralayer interactions and Ising-type interlayer spin couplings.

**Key observables.** Intralayer fluxes, string order parameters, local magnetizations, charge density wave (CDW) order parameter, and flavor-selective density wave (FSDW) order.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of layers (N), intralayer interaction strength (K), interlayer coupling constants (J), and the anisotropy ratio (J'/J).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis uses a mean-field approximation for the interaction terms and assumes a fixed pi-flux gauge sector.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1(a) provides a schematic of a single square layer showing bond types and inequivalent plaquettes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the layered spin-orbital model, performs a parton mapping to an N-flavor Hubbard model, analyzes the emergent SU(N) symmetry, computes the phase diagram for N=3 using mean-field theory, and concludes by mapping mean-field orders back to physical spin-orbital observables like magnetic fragmentation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a family of layered quantum spin-orbital models as a platform to study fractionalization, unconventional forms of symmetry-breaking order, and their possible coexistence. The models are built by stacking $N$ layers of a square-lattice system in which Kitaev-type interactions promote the formation of a $\mathbb{Z}_2$ quantum spin-orbital liquid and coupling the different layers via Ising spin interactions. Using a parton construction, we show how, at low energies, these Hamiltonians can be mapped to $N$-component Fermi Hubbard models on a $π$-flux square lattice at half filling. We also demonstrate that the models acquire an emergent SU($N$) symmetry in the limit of equal all-to-all interlayer couplings and argue that, for $N>2$, the proximity to this limit offers the potential to realize an array of competing phases. To illustrate this point, we compute the zero-temperature phase diagram of the effective $N=3$ Hubbard model within mean-field theory and uncover rich phenomena, including intertwined orders and flavor-selective localization. Mapping back to the original degrees of freedom reveals that the ground states realize distinct forms of magnetic fragmentation, wherein the orbitals remain in a quantum liquid state whereas the spins can present conventional long-range order or nonlocal order characterized by a nontrivial string order parameters. We highlight possible extensions of our construction as well as its potential to provide concrete microscopic models for different fractionalized quantum critical points.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.23894"></a>
### [A Two-Branch Finite-Field Construction for Regular CSS LDPC Bases](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23894v1)

**Authors:** Koki Okada, Kenta Kasai  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23894v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23894_figures/2605.23894_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: FER of the 64-fold lift for the [[n, k, d]] = [[10240, 4108, 10 ≤d ≤32]] CSS code. The hashing line is the depolarizing-channel quantum hashing bound for the effective rate R = 4108/10240. The DE line is an approximate BP density-evolution reference for the regular (3, 10) ensemble.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a systematic algebraic method to construct regular CSS quantum LDPC codes by using finite field cosets to manage parity-check matrix constraints. It demonstrates that this construction can produce large-scale, high-rate codes with excellent error-correction performance and verifiable distance properties.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of constructing regular, finite-length CSS quantum LDPC codes that simultaneously satisfy the CSS commutation requirement, maintain high girth (avoiding short cycles), and suppress low-weight logical operators.

**Main result.** The development of a two-branch multiplicative-coset construction over finite fields that systematically produces regular CSS bases, demonstrated by a high-performance [[10240, 4108, 10 <= d <= 32]] code with an extremely low frame error rate at p=0.058.

**Method.** A two-stage design process involving a finite-field-based base matrix construction to fix degree distribution and initial girth, followed by a cyclic lifting stage using Circulant Permutation Matrices (CPM) subject to algebraic congruence constraints.

**Model / system.** The paper focuses on the construction and decoding of regular Calderbank-Shor-Steane (CSS) quantum low-density parity-check (QLDPC) codes under a depolarizing noise channel.

**Key observables.** Frame Error Rate (FER), minimum distance (d), code rate (R), and girth (specifically the exclusion of 4-cycles and 6-cycles).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Depolarizing probability p = 0.058, code parameters [[10240, 4108, 10 <= d <= 32], and (3, 10)-regularity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The construction relies on sufficient (but not necessarily necessary) algebraic certificates for orthogonality and cycle exclusion; the distance certificate is a lower bound rather than an exact value.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a plot of the Frame Error Rate (FER) versus the depolarizing probability p, compared against the quantum hashing bound and a density evolution reference.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the construction problem, details the two-branch finite-field method for base matrices, describes the algebraic constraints for the lifting stage, provides a concrete high-performance example, and concludes with decoding performance evaluations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This paper develops a two-branch multiplicative-coset construction for regular Calderbank-Shor-Steane (CSS) quantum low-density parity-check base matrices. For a target column weight \(J\) and an even row weight \(L\), the method reduces regularity, CSS orthogonality, and same-type 4-cycle exclusion to explicit quotient-coset conditions over a finite field. A normalized exhaustive search for these conditions produces base matrices for several \((J,L)\) pairs, so the construction is not tied to a single degree distribution. The construction separates the finite-length design into two stages: the base matrix fixes the degree distribution and the first girth constraints, and a cyclic lift randomizes edge connections subject to exact algebraic checks. As a detailed example, we carry one \((3,10)\)-regular base through the lift and decoding stages. For this example, the selected 64-fold lift gives a code whose same-type Tanner graphs have girth at least eight, and it also excludes a specified weight-16 nondegenerate logical-support orbit. The resulting instance is a \([[10240,4108,\,10\le d\le32]]\) CSS code. For decoding, we use joint log-domain belief propagation together with low-complexity deterministic post-processing rules for small residual syndromes, including repairs for residual patterns with two unsatisfied checks. The frame error rate (FER) measurements provide finite-length decoding data for this detailed example; at depolarizing probability \(p=0.058\), the post-processing FER is \(1.0\times10^{-7}\).

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.23442"></a>
### [Ancilla-Efficient QSAMPLE Preparation for Reversible Markov Chains](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23442v1)

**Authors:** Nicholas Zhao  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23442v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23442_figures/2605.23442_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. Structure of the GQSP-based selective-phase compiler e Sϕ(Π0). Top: the block-level gadget (left) equals the circuit C∆,ε(W)†(Pϕ ⊗I)C∆,ε(W) . Bottom: gate-level decomposition, consists of d controlled W gates interleaved with single qubit gates R0, . . . , Rd, which are parametrized by three parameters for higher generality [15]. The second half is the adjoint circuit C∆,ε(W)† with reversed sequence R† d, . . . , R† 0 and controlled W† gates. Here d = O(∆−1 W log(1/εW)).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.23442_figures/2605.23442_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. Architecture of the constant-ancilla QSAMPLE preparation algorithm. Top row: the cooling schedule advances through ℓstages of stationary states |π0⟩→· · · →|πℓ⟩. Bottom row: each transition |eπi⟩→|eπi+1⟩uses the qubitized Szegedy walk, to build a GQSP-based selective-phase compiler, to then use FPAA subroutine to transport the state.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.23442_figures/2605.23442_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3. Fixed-benchmark comparison of the resource cost of our QSAMPLE framework against Wocjan et al. [4]. The benchmark is using an open-boundary ferromagnetic 2 × 4 Ising ladder under random-scan single-site heat-bath Glauber dynamics, with lazified Markov chain Pβi = (I + Gβi)/2, where Gibbs states are annealed through inverse temperatures βi ∈{0.0, 0.3, 0.6, 0.9, 1.2} (ℓ= 4 transitions). The horizontal axis is log10(1/ε), where ε is the trace distance error. Panel A shows our algorithm (blue) achieves lower asymptotic query complexity than [4] (red). Panel B shows the ancilla qubit on our working register remains constant, whilst [4] increases polylogarithmically.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a more efficient quantum algorithm for preparing quantum states that represent the stationary distributions of Markov chains. It achieves a significant reduction in the number of required ancilla qubits and improves the scaling of the computational cost relative to the overlap between states in a cooling schedule.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an efficient quantum algorithm for preparing QSAMPLEs (coherent encodings of stationary distributions of reversible Markov chains) while minimizing ancilla qubit overhead and query complexity.

**Main result.** The authors present an end-to-end framework that reduces the working-register ancilla cost to exactly one qubit and improves the query complexity dependence on the minimum overlap from 1/p_min to 1/sqrt(p_min).

**Method.** The approach utilizes a Selective Phase Compiler built from Generalized Quantum Signal Processing (GQSP) and integrates it with Fixed-Point Amplitude Amplification (FPAA) to rotate through a sequence of stationary states.

**Model / system.** The framework targets reversible, ergodic Markov chains on a finite state space, with a specific application to preparing Gibbs distributions for an Ising ladder model.

**Key observables.** Trace distance (D_tr) and Total Variation Distance (d_TV) between the prepared and target distributions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Minimum overlap (p_min), spectral gap (delta), phase gap (Delta_W), and the number of stages in the cooling schedule (l).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Markov chains must be reversible and ergodic; the algorithm assumes oracle access to the transition matrix and known lower bounds on overlaps and spectral gaps.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the GQSP-based selective-phase compiler structure; Figure 2 illustrates the algorithm's progression; Figure 3 compares the proposed method's query complexity and ancilla overhead against previous frameworks.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QSAMPLE problem, presents the new GQSP-based selective phase compiler, details the integration with FPAA for an end-to-end algorithm, provides a rigorous complexity analysis, and demonstrates application to Gibbs state preparation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Preparing quantum samples (QSAMPLES), coherent encodings of stationary distributions of reversible Markov chains, is a fundamental primitive in quantum sampling, particularly for quantum simulated annealing. A central limitation of existing phase-estimation-based frameworks is the ancilla qubit overhead. In this work, we present a new end-to-end framework requiring only one ancilla qubit in the working register. The key technical ingredient is a selective phase compiler circuit using one ancilla qubit, built from a generalized quantum signal processing (GQSP)-based projector onto the 1-eigenspace of the qubitized Szegedy walk. Embedding these selective phase compilers into the fixed-point amplitude amplification (FPAA) procedure and iterating yields a quantum algorithm that, given an initial state, oracle access, lower bounds on the overlaps between adjacent states, and lower bounds on the phase gaps, outputs a QSAMPLE within any desired trace distance and thus total variation distance. The query complexity scales inversely with the square roots of both the minimum overlap and the minimum spectral gap of the Markov chains across the cooling schedule, up to polylogarithmic factors. We also perform simulations to verify how our qubit and query complexity evolve with the trace distance, and how this work compares to the previous framework. These results establish two improvements over the previous framework by Wocjan and Abeyesinghe. First, the working-register ancilla cost is reduced to one. Second, by inserting our GQSP-based selective phase compiler into the FPAA procedure, we improve the QSAMPLE transport overlap dependence from inverse minimum overlap to inverse square-root minimum overlap, relative to their Grover pi-over-three fixed-point method. Finally, as a direct application, we apply the quantum algorithm to prepare a Gibbs QSAMPLE and obtain a rigorous complexity analysis.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.23591"></a>
### [Asymmetric Scaling Laws from Sparse Features](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23591v1)

**Authors:** John Sous, Michael Winer  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23591v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23591_figures/2605.23591_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Phase diagram in (α1, α2). Solid black lines mark the boundary beyond which the model is well defined. Within it we identify three regimes: symmetric one-exponent scaling, asymmetric two- exponent scaling, and a GD-failure regime with its own scaling law.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.23591_figures/2605.23591_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Scaling collapse and double descent. We plot the rescaled loss ℓBayes ·NαN as a function of (DαD/NαN )1/αN , across a wide range of values for D, N, for fixed (α1 = 1.0, α2 = 0.3). All curves collapse onto a single universal function Sα1,α2, verifying a universal scale-invariant structure consistent with the predicted scaling law. A sharp peak emerges near ξcrit consistent with the double descent phenomenon at the interpolation threshold.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.23591_figures/2605.23591_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Empirical scaling of loss with compute. Each curve corresponds to test loss ℓversus total compute C for a fixed model size N, with C varied by sweeping D, for fixed (α1 = 1.0, α2 = 0.3). The dashed line denotes the predicted compute-optimal scaling ℓ∗(C) ∼C−αC. The empirical loss converges toward this frontier at high compute, just past the double descent spike for each curve demonstrating agreement between theory and experiment.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.23591_figures/2605.23591_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Asymmetry persists under nonlin- earity. Test loss scaling under the ReLU feature map ϕ = σ(ux), trained with Nesterov + adaptive restart; (α1, α2) = (1.0, 0.3), 5 seeds. Top: sparse; bottom: dense. Left: N-sweep at D = 50,000; right: D-sweep at N = 8000. A single exponent α ≈1.5 (dashed lines, jointly fitted with separate intercepts) describes the sparse N-, dense N-, and dense D- sweeps. The sparse D-sweep requires its own shal- lower fit, αD ≈1.2, breaking the dense-baseline sym- metry αN = αD predicted by linear theory.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.23591_figures/2605.23591_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Two-exponent scaling validates linear theory. Test loss scaling under the linear feature map ϕ(x) = ux, computed via the closed-form min- norm least-squares solution; (α1, α2) = (1.0, 0.3), 20 seeds. Top: sparse; bottom: dense. Left: N-sweep at D = 50,000; right: D-sweep at N = 16,000. A single exponent α ≈2.0 (dashed lines, jointly fitted with separate intercepts) describes the sparse N-, dense N-, and dense D-sweeps, in close agreement with the linear-theory prediction αN = α1 + α2 + 1 = 2.30. The sparse D-sweep requires its own shallower fit, αD ≈1.11, matching the predicted sparse exponent αD = (α1 + α2 + 1)/(α1 + 1) = 1.15 and breaking the dense-baseline symmetry αN = αD.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper identifies sparsity as the mechanism behind the asymmetric scaling laws observed in large-scale neural networks. It proves that sparse features cause the error to decay at different rates for model size versus dataset size, suggesting that increasing data is more efficient than increasing model capacity when features are sparse.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Explaining the origin of asymmetric scaling laws in neural networks, where the power-law exponents for model size and dataset size differ due to sparse feature activations.

**Main result.** The authors show that sparsity creates a bottleneck that induces a two-exponent scaling regime and shifts the compute-optimal strategy toward larger datasets rather than larger models.

**Method.** The study uses asymptotic analysis of Bayes-optimal loss, spectral analysis of the feature covariance matrix, and numerical experiments with linear and ReLU-activated random feature models.

**Model / system.** A random feature model where high-dimensional inputs have sparse activations following a power-law distribution and a variance structure controlled by sparsity and decay indices.

**Key observables.** Test loss scaling exponents (alpha_N, alpha_D), probability of gradient descent divergence, and compute-optimal frontier.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sparsity index (alpha_1), variance decay index (alpha_2), model size (N), dataset size (D), and compute budget (C).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes high-dimensional limits (M >> N, D), independent input coordinates, and a fixed random feature map (linear or ReLU).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a phase diagram in the (alpha_1, alpha_2) plane delineating scaling regimes and GD stability; Figure 4 compares test loss scaling for sparse vs. dense models under ReLU activation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the sparse model, derives asymptotic scaling laws for under- and overparameterized regimes, analyzes compute-optimal resource allocation, investigates gradient descent stability, and validates findings via numerical experiments with nonlinear activations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce a model for neural scaling laws under sparse activations. In the model, test loss is often dominated by rare coordinates that are never observed in the training input. This mechanism induces a novel bottleneck absent from dense models. We derive the asymptotic population loss in both the underparameterized and overparameterized regimes, and show that the loss exhibits a double-descent peak near the interpolation threshold -- where the number of parameters is just sufficient to fit the training data -- resulting in a loss curve governed by two distinct scaling exponents -- one for the overparameterized regime and one for the underparameterized regime -- with a gap determined by the degree of sparsity. Additionally, we derive a compute-optimal frontier that favors increasing dataset size over model capacity under fixed compute budgets. We also analyze gradient-descent dynamics and identify a scaling law for the probability that fixed-step gradient descent becomes unstable. We further show that the sparsity-induced effect persists under nonlinear activations.

</details>

<sub>Figure 3 summarizes the QAOA benchmark results, demonstrating that CRiSP consistently achieves higher energy accuracy than the competing approaches across all tasks (see Appendix B for numerical details). **Summary.** This paper introduces CRiSP, an RL-based framework that uses Transformer-guided Monte Carlo Tree Search to efficiently find optimal Clifford gate sequences for initializing variational quantum circuits. By treating state preparation as a sequential decision-making problem, it significantly improves the starting energy for QAOA and VQE, helping to mitigate the effects of barren plateaus.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Variational Quantum Algorithms (VQAs) suffer from optimization bottlenecks like barren plateaus and local minima, and existing Clifford-based initialization methods struggle to scale with large circuit search spaces.

**Main result.** The CRiSP framework outperforms state-of-the-art Clifford initialization methods by a mean of 3.17x in average energy accuracy and 2.44x in best-achieved energy accuracy on QAOA benchmarks.

**Method.** A Reinforcement Learning framework using Neural-Guided Monte Carlo Tree Search (NG-MCTS) with a Transformer-based policy and an incremental horizon curriculum learning strategy.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on Variational Quantum Algorithms, specifically QAOA and VQE, applied to Hamiltonians such as MaxCut, Knapsack, and 1D Ising/XXZ models.

**Key observables.** Ground-state energy (E_opt) and approximation accuracy (E_best / E_opt).

**Important parameters / regimes.** System sizes up to 22 qubits, 1,370 parameters, and various coupling strengths J for the Ising and XXZ models.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes that the optimal initial state can be found by prepending a discrete Clifford prefix to a fixed parameterized rotation architecture.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the VQA initialization workflow; Figure 2 illustrates the CRiSP framework architecture; Figure 3 displays accuracy across QAOA benchmarks; Figure 4 shows training convergence and ablation studies for the curriculum strategy.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the VQA optimization problem, presents the CRiSP RL framework and MCTS implementation, details the curriculum learning strategy, provides numerical benchmarks on QAOA and VQE tasks, and concludes with limitations and future directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Variational Quantum Algorithms (VQAs) potentially offer a pathway to practical quantum advantage, but their optimization is heavily hindered by barren plateaus and numerous local minima. While classically simulable Clifford circuits can warm-start VQAs to accelerate convergence, existing heuristic-based initialization methods struggle to scale within vast combinatorial search spaces. To overcome this bottleneck, we propose CRiSP (a Clifford Reinforcement Learning agent for State Preparation), a framework that formulates discrete prefix selection as a sequential decision-making problem. CRiSP utilizes Neural-Guided Monte Carlo Tree Search, driven by a Transformer-based policy trained via self-play, to insert learned Clifford gates before fixed parameterized rotations. This enables the construction of high-quality initial states entirely through polynomial-time classical stabilizer simulation without altering the underlying circuit architecture. By integrating a curriculum learning strategy that progressively expands the search horizon, the agent efficiently scales to deep circuits. Evaluated on QAOA benchmarks of up to $22$ qubits and $1{,}370$ parameters, CRiSP outperforms state-of-the-art Clifford initialization methods by a mean of $3.17\times$ (max $45.02\times$) in average energy accuracy and $2.44\times$ (max $16.01\times$) in best-achieved energy accuracy. Assessments on VQE tasks further demonstrate the framework's robustness and generalizability.

</details>

### [Exact solution of generalized gauge-invariant Ising chains with multi-spin interactions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23228v1)

**Authors:** Pavel Khrapov, Stepan Shchurenkov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23228v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.23228_figures/2605.23228_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Interlayer interaction</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>FIG. 3: Transformation of the three-chain gauge-invariant model to the two-chain Ising model.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.23228_figures/2605.23228_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Transformation of the four-chain gauge-invariant model to the three-chain Ising model.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.23228_figures/2605.23228_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5: String tension (5a) hamiltonian (85) with βl ∈[0.001, 1] and βt ∈[0, 1] and plots (5b) with βt ∈[0, 1] , βl = 0.01, 0.2, 0.5, 0.75.</sub>

<img src="2605.23228_figures/2605.23228_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
