<sub>Fig. 5 Brillouin zone grid convergence. (a) TRTA(N) (blue circles) and TfW(N) (red squares) vs. BZ grid density N. (b) Normalised correction δTfW/(TRTA −T0) vs. N; grey band marks the asymptote 11.1 ± 0.3% (N ≥11). The non- monotone behaviour at N = 7, 9 reflects the Γ-centred Monkhorst–Pack sampling artefact.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new numerical solver for the phonon Boltzmann Transport Equation that accurately includes the full scattering matrix without the massive computational cost usually required. It achieves this by exploiting the fact that the non-equilibrium part of the phonon distribution is low-dimensional, even when the scattering operator itself is complex. This enables more precise thermal modeling in nanoscale transistors.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The Relaxation-Time Approximation (RTA) in the phonon Boltzmann Transport Equation (BTE) fails to account for intermode energy redistribution, but incorporating the full scattering matrix is computationally prohibitive due to its high-rank, incompressible nature.

**Main result.** The authors discover that while the scattering matrix is incompressible, the non-equilibrium phonon distribution exists in a low-dimensional subspace, allowing for an efficient 3D BTE solver that captures a 11.2% correction to temperature rise over RTA.

**Method.** A deterministic 3D BTE solver using a finite-volume discretization and a hybrid architecture that combines dense spatial sweeps with low-rank SVD-based truncation of the in-scattering operator.

**Model / system.** Steady-state phonon Boltzmann Transport Equation (BTE) for nanoscale semiconductor structures, such as FinFET-like geometries, including three-phonon Normal and Umpland processes.

**Key observables.** Effective thermal conductivity, peak temperature rise, non-equilibrium distribution deviation, and transport selectivity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Brillouin zone refinement levels, Knudsen number, device length (fin length), and scattering matrix rank.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The phonon transport is modeled in the ballistic and quasi-ballistic regimes using a linearized BTE and the Stillinger-Weber potential for scattering matrix elements.

**Figures summary.** The paper includes tables comparing scattering matrix properties (rank, spectral flatness), convergence tests for BZ grid density, and validation of temperature predictions in 3D FinFET structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the limitations of RTA, analyzes the incompressibility of the scattering matrix, proposes a low-rank solution-based solver, validates the method against 1D analytic solutions, and demonstrates application to 3D nanoscale devices.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Solutions to the phonon Boltzmann transport equation under the relaxation-time approximation (RTA) are fundamentally limited in that they do not account for the off-diagonal elements of the scattering matrix, which encode intermode energy redistribution. We find that the phonon in-scattering matrix is globally incompressible, requiring nearly its full rank for any useful Frobenius accuracy. The incompressibility worsens as the Brillouin zone is refined. We show that, despite this difficulty, one can develop a computationally efficient 3D BTE solver incorporating the complete scattering matrix by leveraging our two structural discoveries: the non-equilibrium phonon distribution inhabits a remarkably low-dimensional subspace of mode space regardless of how many phonon modes exist, and the leading singular modes of the scattering operator align selectively with this transport-active subspace. Consequently, truncation incurs negligible transport error even under large norm error. The phonon streaming operator's mode-diagonal character further motivates a hybrid architecture that exploits these two properties. When applied to nanoscale structures emulating a fin field-effect transistor, our BTE solver quantifies a geometry-independent multiplicative correction to the temperature rise predicted under RTA. Our theoretical work and resulting BTE solver help enable rigorous study of phonon transport and systematic design of devices and structures in the ballistic and quasi-ballistic phonon transport regime.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.23725"></a>
### [Nonreciprocal conductance in uniformly dissipative devices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23725v1)

**Authors:** Oliver Solow, Emil J. Bergholtz, Karsten Flensberg  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.23725v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>FIG. 1. (a)A schematic of the nanowire in red connected to the leads in yellow. The magnetic field ⃗B is at some skewed angle compared to the spin-orbit direction. (b) Band struc- ture of Rashba nanowire with skewed magnetic field, with the Fermi level marked in black. The Fermi velocity is given by the slope of the dispersion at the Fermi level. This slope is sig- nificantly larger for the right-movers than for the left-movers.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>FIG. 2. The nonreciprocity parameter ∆G as a function of chemical potential and Zeeman field for a dissipative Rashba nanowire of length L = 101 at two different dissipation strengths. The green lines are the eigenenergies of Eq.(8), and the magenta dot is the parameters for Figure 3. </details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.23725_figures/2605.23725_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. ∆G as a function of γ. The blue line is the numerical results, the dashed line is from a fit to (17). Parameters are as in Fig. 2, and B = 0.2, ω = 1.2.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that uniformly dissipative electronic circuits can exhibit nonreciprocal conductance due to asymmetric transmission times. By studying a Rashba nanowire with a skewed magnetic field, the authors show how interference and band structure asymmetry drive this effect. The study highlights that dissipation can qualitatively change the sign of conductance asymmetry.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a mechanism for nonreciprocal transport in non-Hermitian systems that does not rely on the Non-Hermitian Skin Effect, which is crucial for understanding transport in open quantum systems and dephasing in electronic circuits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how uniform dissipation (non-Hermitian effects) affects measurable quantities like conductance, specifically determining the conditions for nonreciprocal conductance (G_LR != G_RL).

**Main result.** The authors show that nonreciprocal conductance arises from differences in transmission times between left- and right-moving electrons due to asymmetric dispersion relations, and that this effect can change sign depending on dissipation strength.

**Method.** The study uses an analytical approach relating the S-matrix of a two-terminal system to a multi-terminal system via frequency analytical continuation, supplemented by numerical simulations using the Quantica software package.

**Model / system.** A ballistic Rashba nanowire with a skewed magnetic field that breaks both inversion and time-reversal symmetry, coupled weakly to a third 'featureless' lead that introduces uniform dissipation.

**Key observables.** Nonreciprocal conductance difference (Delta G = G_LR - G_RL) and transmission times (tau_LR, tau_RL).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dissipation strength (gamma), magnetic field strength (B), chemical potential (mu), and Rashba spin-orbit coupling (alpha).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the weak dissipation limit (gamma -> 0) and assumes the dissipation is uniform across the device.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1(a) shows the nanowire setup with a third lead; Fig 1(b) shows the asymmetric dispersion relation; Fig 2 displays the conductance difference as a function of system parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of nonreciprocity in dissipative systems, presents a general model of a Rashba nanowire, develops the theoretical link between conductance and transmission times, and then analyzes the dependence of the results on dissipation strength and magnetic field.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

When studying non-Hermitian electronic systems, an obvious question is how various non-Hermitian effects affect measurable quantities like the conductance. Here, we show that uniformly dissipative circuits can exhibit nonreciprocal conductance, meaning that the two nonlocal conductances are different. We describe how this happens through a difference in transmission times between left-moving and right-moving electrons. We consider a specific case of a dissipative Rashba nanowire with a skewed magnetic field, and show how this difference in transmission times comes about through interference inside the circuit, and how this is modified as the dissipation strength changes.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.23646"></a>
### [Graphene-based Photodetector with Engineered Hot Carrier Cooling Dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23646v1)

**Authors:** Yishu Huang, Anand Nivedan, Florian Ludwig, Bohai Liu, Michiel Debaets, Steven Brems, Hai I. Wang, Alessandro Principi, Dries Van Thourhout, Christian Haffner, Aron W. Cummings, Klaas-Jan Tielrooij

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a method to enhance graphene photodetectors by using a graphite layer to screen Coulomb interactions, which slows down hot-carrier cooling. This 'trapping' of heat leads to a significantly improved photothermoelectric response. The findings suggest a scalable way to improve integrated photonics components through engineered carrier dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of increasing the photoresponsivity of graphene-based photodetectors by controlling the cooling dynamics of hot carriers.

**Main result.** Introducing a graphite layer for proximity screening suppresses carrier-carrier scattering, increasing the hot-carrier cooling time by up to a factor of four and enhancing internal photoresponsivity by approximately 50%.

**Method.** The researchers used a heterodyne photomixing technique with two continuous-wave lasers to modulate absorbed power and measure the resulting carrier temperature oscillations via a lock-in amplifier.

**Model / system.** A waveguide-integrated photodetector consisting of a WSe2-graphene-WSe2 van der Waals heterostructure placed on a silicon optical waveguide, with an optional nearby graphite layer for proximity screening.

**Key observables.** Hot-carrier cooling time (tau), internal photoresponsivity, and photovoltage (V_delta).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cooling time increase (2-4x), photoresponsivity enhancement (1.5x), wavelength (1550 nm), and the distance (d) between graphene and graphite.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The cooling is dominated by intrinsic optical phonon relaxation rather than hyperbolic phonon-mediated cooling due to the use of WSe2; the optical mode profile remains largely unchanged by the screening layer.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the device schematic, top/cross-section views, and the physical mechanism of proximity screening. Figure 2 displays the Lorentzian roll-off of the photovoltage response and the extracted cooling time versus power. Supplementary figures include material quality verification via Raman and AFM.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of graphene photodetector performance, describes the device architecture and the engineering of the screening layer, details the photomixing experimental setup, presents the results showing enhanced cooling times and responsivity, and concludes with a discussion on the physical mechanism and future scaling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Graphene has emerged as a promising material for integration into silicon photonics, owing to its ultrafast and broadband photoresponse without the need for an external bias voltage. This photoresponse relies on the photo-thermoelectric effect created by hot carriers. A key factor underlying the performance of graphene photodetectors is the cooling dynamics of these hot carriers. In this work, we engineer these dynamics in a WSe2-graphene-WSe2 waveguide-integrated photodetector. In particular, by introducing proximity screening by a nearby graphite layer to this structure, we prolong the hot-carrier cooling time, leading to an enhanced photoresponse. We characterize the cooling dynamics under continuous-wave laser excitation by employing a photomixing technique, revealing an increase in the cooling time by up to a factor of four. Direct photoresponse measurements show that the internal photoresponsivity improves by approximately 50%. Together, these results demonstrate the potential of proximity screening to enhance the performance of graphene-based photodetectors on an integrated photonics platform.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how the alignment of two moiré interfaces in trilayer 2D heterostructures leads to 'angle-locking' via commensuration torques. It demonstrates that the overlap of favorable stacking regions stabilizes specific twist angles, providing a mechanism for controlling the structural and mechanical properties of moiré superlattices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the energetic origins and stability of commensuration-driven torques and angle-locking in double-moiré trilayer heterostructures.

**Main result.** The study identifies that double-moiré commensuration creates local energy minima and restoring torques due to the enhanced overlap of low-energy stacking domains, with graphene/hBN systems potentially exhibiting global energy minima at specific angles.

**Method.** Large-scale atomistic relaxations using the LAMMPS molecular dynamics package with various interatomic potentials (Tersoff, REBO2, and DRIP force-fields) and electrostatic models.

**Model / system.** Trilayer heterostructures including twisted trilayer hBN (t3BN) homostructures and graphene/hBN heterolayers (e.g., G/BN/G, G/BN/BN) with varying twist angles and lattice mismatches.

**Key observables.** Total energy per atom, torque (angular derivative of energy), binding energy, and stacking configuration maps.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angles (theta_12, theta_32), lattice mismatch, torque constants, and electrostatic interaction types (short-range vs. long-range).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Use of commensurate approximants to allow for periodic boundary conditions in incommensurate phases.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows energy maps and energy variation vs. twist angle; Figure 2 illustrates local stacking configuration maps; Figure 3 displays sliding-dependent energy maps and binding energy trends for G/BN/G and related systems; Figure 4 shows total energy per atom evolution.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of double-moiré stability, details the atomistic simulation methodology, presents results for t3BN homostructures, extends the analysis to graphene/hBN heterostructures, and discusses the role of electrostatic interactions and superlubricity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study commensuration-driven torques and angle locking in double-moiré trilayer hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) and graphene heterostructures using large-scale atomistic relaxations. In twisted trilayer hBN (t3BN) homostructures, double-moiré commensuration ($θ_{12} = -θ_{23}$) give rise to local energy minima accompanied by torque sign reversals, signaling a restoring tendency toward the commensurate configuration. The corresponding binding energies are $\sim$0.2-0.3 meV/atom, originating from enhanced overlap of low-energy stacking domains, although the system is globally stable at zero twist. In contrast, in graphene/hBN heterolayers systems the global energy minimum can coincide with the double-moiré commensuration angle, particularly near $\sim$0.6$^{\circ}$, reflecting competition between lattice mismatch and interfacial relaxation. Incommensurate atomic structures have reduced stabilization due to suppressed overlap of low-energy stacking and have enhanced superlubricity due to spatial averaging of interfacial energies. These results establish double-moiré commensuration as a general, system-dependent mechanism for twist-angle stabilization, whose angular stability is characterized by the torque magnitude and binding energy. Coulomb electrostatic interactions further enhance the stabilization energy without changing the underlying physics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Non-reciprocal Coulomb drag in a ballistic quantum wire](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22945v1)

**Summary.** The researchers found that even in ballistic quantum wires, a significant non-reciprocal Coulomb drag signal persists. They attribute this to low-energy disorder that breaks translational symmetry, challenging existing models that predict minimal non-reciprocity in pristine wires.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study explores the interplay between electron-electron interactions (Luttinger liquid physics) and disorder-induced rectification, which is relevant to understanding many-body dynamics in 1D systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the origin of the unexpectedly large non-reciprocal Coulomb drag component in nearly pristine, ballistic 1D quantum wires.

**Main result.** A non-reciprocal drag component comparable in strength to the reciprocal component is detected, likely arising from low-energy disorder/potential barriers that break translational symmetry.

**Method.** 1D Coulomb drag measurements were performed using a constant AC drive current and lock-in amplification in a GaAs/AlGaAs heterostructure device.

**Model / system.** A 7.2-micron long quasi-1D device with two parallel channels (one ballistic, one non-ballistic) created via Ti/Au top gates in a 2DEG.

**Key observables.** Drag voltage (reciprocal/antisymmetric and non-reciprocal/symmetric components), conductance (G), and temperature/bias voltage dependence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (down to 30 mK), bias voltage, Luttinger liquid interaction parameter (Kc), and characteristic energy scales (Tc, T0).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The estimation of the interaction parameter assumes a specific model for Coulomb potential and 1D density; simple barrier transmission models are insufficient for a full description.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows drag mechanisms and device schematics; Fig 2 shows conductance plateaus and gate voltage dependence under varying temperature and bias.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the drag mechanisms, describes the experimental device and measurement setup, presents conductance and drag data, analyzes temperature/bias dependence using various disorder models, and discusses the implications of the findings.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

1D-Coulomb drag serves as a platform for probing electron-electron interactions in 1D systems. Under the charge fluctuation formalism, the non-reciprocal component of Coulomb drag signal in mesoscopic devices is predicted to rely on the breaking of translational invariance due to intrinsic disorder. In this work, we report the measurement of a Coulomb drag device with a ballistic drag wire, allowing us to study the drag signal in nearly pristine quantum wires. Surprisingly, a non-reciprocal component with strength comparable to that of the reciprocal component is still detected across the measured regime, despite the drag wire being ballistic. We suggest that the non-reciprocal signal arises from low energy disorder in the device, which is consistent with the evolution of the drag wire's conductance at low biases voltages and temperatures. Additionally, the non-reciprocal component of the drag signal shows a power-law temperature dependence that coincides with a diffusive model, while the reciprocal component's temperature dependence cannot be explained under the existing framework. The bias voltage dependence of the drag wire conductance is fitted into three models to extract the interaction parameter and disorder level within the wire, but none of the models provides a fully self-consistent explanation for the data.

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>FIG. 3. (a-b) CPRs of the AMJJ at different disorder strength W. Panel (a) demonstrates φ phase before the transition and 0 phase after the transition. The AM junction length is of Lx = 18. Panel (b) demonstrates φ phase before transition and π phase after transition. The AM junction length is of Lx = 14. (c-d) Corresponding harmonic components in CPRs as a function of W, corresponding to Fig. 2(c) and 2(d), respectively. Other parameters are the same as in Fig. 2.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how disorder affects the stability of exotic superconducting phases in altermagnetic Josephson junctions. It finds that disorder can drive transitions between 0, pi, and phi phases by suppressing higher-order harmonics and reducing effective spin-splitting. These findings are crucial for understanding the potential for using altermagnets in functional superconducting devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether the exotic pi and phi phases in altermagnetic Josephson junctions (AMJJs) are stable against the presence of disorder found in real materials.

**Main result.** Disorder induces phase transitions between the pi and 0 phases and causes the anomalous phi phase to be highly fragile, eventually collapsing into 0 or pi phases. This is driven by the suppression of higher-order harmonics in the current-phase relation and the reduction of effective spin-split strength.

**Method.** The authors use a mean-field tight-binding Hamiltonian and the recursive Green's function method to calculate supercurrents, averaging results over thousands of disorder configurations.

**Model / system.** A two-dimensional d-wave altermagnetic Josephson junction consisting of two s-wave superconductor leads separated by a disordered d-wave altermagnet region.

**Key observables.** Current-phase relation (CPR), critical supercurrent (Ic), and Fourier harmonic amplitudes (I1h, I2h, I3h).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Disorder strength (W), junction length (Lx), altermagnetic hopping amplitude (tJ), superconducting gap (Delta), and temperature (T).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a 2D square lattice and uses a mean-field approximation for the superconducting pairing.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the transition of the CPR from phi to 0 or pi phases and a Fourier analysis demonstrating the decay of higher-order harmonics.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the altermagnetic Josephson junction system, describes the tight-binding model and disorder implementation, presents numerical results regarding phase transitions and supercurrent suppression, and concludes with the physical implications of disorder on phase stability.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Altermagnetic Josephson junctions (AMJJs) can host unconventional $π$ phase and $\varphi$ phase despite vanishing net magnetizations. Whether these phases are stable against disorder existing in real materials remains an open question. Here, we investigate impact of disorder on exotic phases in two-dimensional d-wave AMJJs. We show that disorder is able to induce phase transitions between the exotic $π$ and conventional 0 phases, accompanied by a strong suppression of critical current. This behavior is attributed to modifications of the tunneling Cooper-pair phase shift and superconducting decoherence. Remarkably, the anomalous $\varphi$ phase is highly fragile in presence of disorder and can be driven to either a $π$ phase or 0 phase in a nonreciprocal manner. Across such transitions, the first harmonic of current-phase relation changes sign, while the higher-order harmonics are rapidly suppressed. Our findings reveal the crucial role of disorder in tailoring distinct phases of AMJJs and shed new light on their potential functionalities.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.23577"></a>
### [Momentum-Resolved Tunneling Modulation Induced Giant Multistate Resistance in Antiferroelectric Multiferroic Junction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23577v1)

**Authors:** Wei Yang, Yibo Xu, Shen Li, Jiangchao Han, Jiayou Chen, Juan-Carlos Rojas-Sánchez, Stéphane Mangin, Xiaoyang Lin, Weisheng Zhao

<img src="2605.23577_figures/2605.23577_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new way to design multiferroic memory by using antiferroelectric transitions to modulate tunneling through momentum-space redistribution. This approach overcomes the traditional conflict between high-performance readout and low-power writing, achieving massive resistance changes at room temperature.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The fundamental trade-off in multiferroic tunnel junctions (MFTJs) where strong electric fields required for high tunnel electroresistance (TER) simultaneously impede efficient ferroelectric polarization switching.

**Main result.** The discovery of a dual mechanism in an antiferroelectric (AFE) junction that achieves giant TER (~7.6e3%) and TMR (>6.8e5%) by modulating momentum-resolved evanescent decay rates rather than just barrier height, enabling six non-volatile resistance states.

**Method.** First-principles calculations combining Density Functional Theory (DFT) with the Non-Equability Green's Function (NEGF) formalism.

**Model / system.** A van der Waals heterostructure consisting of ferromagnetic Fe3GaTe2 electrodes and a bilayer alpha-In2Se3 antiferroelectric/ferroelectric barrier.

**Key observables.** Tunneling electroresistance (TER), tunneling magnetoresistance (TMR), spin-resolved transmission spectra, and momentum-resolved decay rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Room temperature stability, six distinct resistance states, and switching barriers (approx. 34-37 meV for AFE vs 77 meV for FE).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Use of GGA-PBE functional with DFT-D3 dispersion corrections and a Hubbard U=0 approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares FE and AFE switching; Figure 2 shows atomic stacking and electrostatic profiles; Figure 3 presents spin polarization, TMR, and TER spectra; Figure 4 shows LDOS distributions; Figure 5 displays k-dependent decay rate distributions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the MFTJ trade-off, describes the Fe3GaTe2/alpha-In2Se3/Fe3GaTe2 heterostructure, details the DFT+NEGF methodology, presents results on transport properties and momentum-resolved modulation, and concludes with the implications for multi-state memory.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Multiferroic tunnel junctions (MFTJs), integrating ferroelectric and ferromagnetic functionalities within a single nanoscale device, hold significant promise for non-volatile, multi-state memory and innovative computing paradigms. In conventional MFTJs, tunneling resistance modulation relies primarily on ferroelectric (FE) polarization switching, which alters interfacial electric fields and shifts the Fermi level of adjacent ferromagnetic electrodes. However, achieving high tunnelelectroresistance (TER) through this approach demands strong built-in electric fields, which simultaneously hinder FE polarization switching, creating an intrinsic trade-off between reliable data reading and efficient writing. Here, we propose a dual mechanism that combines antiferroelectric (AFE) phase-transition modulation of the evanescent decay states with interfacial spin filtering based on $Fe_3GaTe_2$/bilayer-$In_2Se_3$/$Fe_3GaTe_2$ heterostructure. Beyond altering the electrostatic potential as in AFE-FE switching, the transitions between head-type and tail-type AFE states preserve the centrosymmetric potential profile yet fundamentally modulate the momentum-resolved distribution of evanescent decay rates across the Brillouin zone. When integrated with perfect spin filtering at the $Fe_3GaTe_2$/$α$-$In_2Se_3$ interface, this mechanism yields a giant TER (~$7.6\times10^3\%$), over 4 times that of conventional FE-based MFTJs, and a TMR exceeding $6.8\times10^5\%$, enhanced by two orders of magnitude over typical MFTJs. These mechanisms resolve the performance trade-off in MFTJs, enabling six distinct non-volatile resistance states at room temperature.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.23001"></a>
### [Nonlinear Wave Propagation in 1D Polycatenated Ring Chains](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23001v1)

**Summary.** This paper investigates nonlinear wave propagation in chains of interlocked rings, showing that unlike rigid granular crystals, these systems have tunable nonlinearity and intrinsic dispersion. By adjusting the geometry of the rings, one can control the wave speed and the nonlinear exponent, creating a designable platform for nonlinear dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Characterizing nonlinear wave dynamics in 1D chains of polycatenated (interlocked) rings and understanding how structural flexibility and geometry tune the system's nonlinearity.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrated that polycatenated rings support nonlinear waves with a compact wavefront and trailing oscillations due to energy partitioning into bending modes, and that the nonlinearity exponent can be tuned via ring aspect ratio and contact angle.

**Method.** The study combines dynamic impact experiments with finite-element analysis (FEA) and discrete-particle simulations using both a dual-spring model and a simplified power-law model.

**Model / system.** A 1D vertical chain of interlocked stainless steel tori pretensioned by gravity, modeled as a discrete-particle system with nonlinear Hertzian contact and linear structural bending.

**Key observables.** Force-time profiles, wave speed (Vs), waveform morphology (leading wavefront vs. trailing oscillations), and effective contact exponent (n).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ring aspect ratio (R/r), contact angle (theta), effective stiffness (K), effective mass (meff), and gravitational pretension.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is treated as a continuous medium using a long-wavelength approximation; the contact is modeled as Hertzian; and the rings are assumed to be in a perpendicular orientation.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the experimental setup and the dual-spring/power-law models; Fig 2 shows FEA results for different aspect ratios; Fig 4 compares experimental wave speeds to theoretical predictions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the polycatenated system, describes the experimental and numerical models, presents results on waveform morphology and energy partitioning, demonstrates geometric tunability of nonlinearity, and provides an analytical derivation of wave speed scaling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the nonlinear wave dynamics of one-dimensional chains of polycatenated rings. These interlocked structures support amplitude-dependent nonlinear wave propagation driven by tensile activation and internal structural flexibility, unlike traditional granular crystals. Through dynamic impact experiments, finite-element modeling, and discrete-particle simulations of vertical chains pretensioned by gravity, we observe and explain nonlinear waves characterized by a compact leading wavefront followed by persistent trailing oscillations, which arise from energy partitioning into the rings' internal bending modes. Further, we demonstrate that the system's nonlinearity is not a fixed material constant. By altering the rings' geometric aspect ratio and contact angles, we can tune the effective contact exponent and the amplitude scaling of the wave speed. This work builds upon nonlinear wave propagation in classical granular crystals and establishes polycatenated systems as a highly tunable and designable platform to study and control nonlinear dynamics.

</details>

<img src="2605.23211_figures/2605.23211_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>

**Summary.** This paper explores how the Quantum Anomalous Hall effect influences the light-matter interaction in 2D materials. It identifies a sharp transition in optical transparency at the bandgap energy and demonstrates that these optical properties can be used to accurately measure the material's bandgap.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the frequency-dependent optical properties (transmission, reflection, and absorption) of 2D materials exhibiting the Quantum Anomalous Hall effect.

**Main result.** The optical response shows a universal behavior depending on the ratio of photon energy to bandgap energy, featuring a singular point of total reflection at the bandgap threshold and recovering graphene-like behavior as the gap vanishes.

**Method.** The authors use the Kubo formalism to derive optical conductivities and apply Maxwell's equations with boundary conditions at the 2D sheet to calculate electromagnetic coefficients.

**Model / system.** A two-band model of a gapped 2D material with intrinsic time-reversal symmetry breaking, characterized by longitudinal and antisymmetric Hall conductivities.

**Key observables.** Transmission (T), reflection (R), and absorption (A) coefficients.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Scaled frequency (zeta = h-bar*omega/2m), bandgap energy (2m), and the fine-structure constant (alpha).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Low temperatures where thermal fluctuations are negligible, an isotropic 2D sheet, and a focus on universal scaling rather than non-universal material parameters.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows optical conductivities vs. scaled frequency; Figure 2 displays the evolution of T, R, and A coefficients; Figure 3 illustrates the singular jump in the reflection coefficient at the threshold.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical problem, defines the conductivity model using the Kubo formalism, derives the electromagnetic response coefficients, analyzes different frequency regimes (insulating vs. metallic), and discusses the limits of vanishing gaps.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the optical properties of gapped two-dimensional materials which are subject to the quantum anomalous Hall effect. At sufficiently low temperatures the transmission, reflection and absorption coefficients are found to have a universal behavior that depends only on the ratio of the photonic energy and the gap energy. There is a singular behavior with total reflection when these energies are equal. In the limit of a vanishing gap we recover results for graphene, where the optical coefficients depend only on the fine-structure constant. The observed optical properties provide an accurate measurement of the bandgap.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper uses polarization-dependent ARPES and DFT to reveal the orbital-selective electronic structure of the magnetic topological semimetal EuAgAs. It identifies Dirac-like states and a van Hove singularity that are robust against antiferromagnetic ordering. The findings provide crucial insight into how orbital symmetry and magnetism interplay in topological materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to investigate the electronic structure, orbital character, and the impact of antiferromagnetic ordering on the low-energy states of the magnetic topological semimetal candidate EuAgAs.

**Main result.** The researchers identified orbital-selective Dirac-like states and a higher-order van Hove singularity, finding that the low-energy electronic structure remains largely unaffected by the antiferromagnetic transition at 12 K.

**Method.** The study combines polarization-dependent and photon-energy-dependent ARPES with Density Functional Theory (DFT) calculations using the VASP package and Wannier90.

**Model / system.** The physical system is EuAgAs, a magnetic topological Dirac semimetal candidate with a hexagonal ZrBeSi-type structure and an antiferromagnetic ground state.

**Key observables.** Fermi surface mapping, constant energy contours, band dispersions, spectral intensity variations under different polarizations, and magnetic susceptibility.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Néel temperature (TN) approx 12 K, photon energies ranging from 9 to 32 eV, and temperature range from 9 K to 30 K.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the validity of the DFT+U formalism for Eu 4f states and uses the GGA-PBE functional with spin-orbit coupling.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows magnetic structure and calculated bands; Fig 2 compares experimental and theoretical Fermi surfaces; Fig 3 illustrates 3D band dispersions and van Hove singularities; Fig 4 shows temperature-dependent FS maps; Fig 5 demonstrates orbital-selective intensity modulation via polarization.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the material and its magnetic properties, presents experimental ARPES results (Fermi surface and dispersion), compares these with DFT calculations to identify orbital character, investigates the 3D nature of the bands, and discusses the stability of the electronic states across the magnetic transition.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Magnetic topological semimetals provide a promising platform for emergent quantum phenomena driven by the interplay between magnetism and relativistic fermions, including anomalous transport effects and tunable topological phases. Here, we investigate the electronic structure and orbital character of EuAgAs, a magnetic topological Dirac semimetal candidate, using density functional theory (DFT) and polarization dependent angle resolved photoemission spectroscopy (ARPES). Fermi surface mapping and constant energy contours measured at 9 eV reveal ring like features that systematically expand with increasing binding energy, consistent with nearly linear low energy Dirac like dispersion. ARPES measurements at different photon energies hint at the presence of a van Hove singularity predicted by DFT calculations. Furthermore, this indicates that the photoemission matrix elements are highly sensitive to the excitation energy, allowing different photon energies to selectively probe distinct orbital characters. Polarization dependent ARPES measurements performed in s- and p-polarized geometries exhibit pronounced variations in spectral intensity, indicating symmetry selective orbital contributions to electronic states. These matrix element driven intensity modulations are well reproduced by DFT calculations. Furthermore, the observed Dirac like states remain nearly unchanged over the temperature range from 9 K to 30 K, suggesting that the magnetic ordering has minimal influence on the electronic structure. Our combined experimental and theoretical results provide detailed insight into the orbital selective electronic structure of EuAgAs and its implications for magnetic topological quantum states.

</details>

<sub>FIG. 3: Magnetic-field-driven superconductor-insulator transition. a, Sheet resistance versus magnetic field measured in a current-bias configuration (0.1–1 nA) for samples DPRes9 and DPRes13 at temperatures ranging from 10 mK (blue) to 400 mK (red) in 100 mK steps. The anomalous kink in the 10 mK curve of DPRes9 is reproducible and could originate from low-temperature sensitivity to inhomogeneous current paths [23] in the narrow strip of the mesa Hall bar. b, Sheet resistance of the same samples measured in voltage bias, together with the corresponding superfluid stiffness (blue triangles and green circles) extracted from the on-chip resonators, as a function of magnetic field. The...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.23838_figures/2605.23838_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Positive Kerr nonlinearity and dissipation. a, c, Microwave transmission S21, in arbitrary units, as a function of photon number and frequency shift δf/f relative to the low-photon-number resonance frequency, at B = 0 T (a) and B = 0.3 T (c), for sample DPRes8. b, Relative frequency shift δf/f versus photon number, extracted at various magnetic fields. d, Internal quality factor Qi versus photon number, at various magnetic fields; the legend is the same as in b. In b and d, filled black circles correspond to measurements performed after zero-field cooling, whereas the open black circle corresponds to a measurement with trapped vortices. Measurements were performed at T = 0.01 K.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that in strongly disordered superconductors, the superfluid density is unexpectedly resilient to magnetic fields, decaying only logarithmically due to a collective pinning effect. It identifies a continuous quantum critical point at the transition to the insulator. The discovery of a positive Kerr effect in the vortex glass also suggests potential applications in quantum sensing.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study explores the dynamics of a many-body system (vortex glass) subject to competing random potentials and long-range interactions, which is highly relevant to the study of localization and collective dynamics in disordered many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the mechanism behind the magnetic-field-driven superconductor-insulator transition (SIT) in strongly disordered superconductors and how the vortex glass state influences the collapse of superfluid stiffness.

**Main result.** The superfluid density decreases only logarithmically with the magnetic field due to a collective vortex-pinning mechanism where vortex-vortex interactions enhance pinning, eventually vanishing linearly at a continuous quantum critical point.

**Method.** The study combines plasmonic microwave spectroscopy of superconducting resonators with simultaneous DC transport measurements and numerical simulations of 2D logarithmically interacting particles in a random potential.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of amorphous indium oxide (a:InO) thin films in a high-disorder, high-magnetic-field regime, modeled as a 2D assembly of vortices in a random pinning potential with logarithmic interactions.

**Key observables.** Superfluid stiffness (extracted from kinetic inductance), sheet resistance, critical current density, internal quality factor, and microwave transmission (S21).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic field (B), critical field (Bc), disorder strength (W), temperature (T ~ 10 mK), and vortex density (n/nc).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The London approximation for vortex interactions fails at high densities; the system is treated as effectively 2D due to the thin film geometry.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the resonator device and microwave transmission; Figure 2 displays superfluid stiffness decay across different disorder levels; Figure 3 shows magnetoresistance isotherms and the linear approach to the critical field; Figure 4 illustrates the positive Kerr nonlinearity and power-dependent dissipation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of the SIT, presents experimental microwave spectroscopy results showing anomalous logarithmic decay, proposes a 'cage model' for interaction-enhanced pinning, validates this with numerical simulations of vortex glasses, and concludes with observations of nonlinear electromagnetic responses.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The interplay between disorder and vortex--vortex interactions in strongly disordered superconductors in a magnetic field can stabilize a vortex-glass state, characterized by strong pinning and the absence of positional order. Yet its role in the destruction of superconductivity at the field-driven superconductor--insulator transition has remained unresolved. Here we use plasmonic microwave spectroscopy of superconducting resonators patterned from amorphous indium oxide thin films to directly track the superfluid density up to the critical field $B_c$. We find an unexpected resilience of the superfluid density, which decreases only logarithmically over nearly three orders of magnitude in field, in stark contrast to the rapid power-law suppression expected for vortex lattices. We attribute this anomalously slow decay to a collective vortex-pinning mechanism counterintuitively enhanced by vortex--vortex interactions. The superfluid density then vanishes linearly at $B_c$, where independent magnetoresistance measurements identify a continuous quantum critical point, unlike the abrupt transition observed at zero field. We further uncover an exceptionally large nonlinear electromagnetic response of the vortex glass, manifested as a pronounced positive-Kerr effect with potential for quantum sensing. These results show how disorder controls the critical magnetic field and identify the vortex glass as the key intermediate state governing the magnetic-field-induced superconductor--insulator transition.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Self-organized formation of step-terrace structure in SrRuO3 thin films grown on mixed-terminated SrTiO3 (100) substrates](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23450v1)

**Authors:** Ryotaro Arakawa, Sachio Komori, Kotaro Tomita, Shunsei Komori, Masaaki A. Tanaka, Tomoyasu Taniyama

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that SrRuO3 thin films can spontaneously form well-defined step-terrace structures when grown on untreated SrTiO3 substrates. By controlling growth parameters like temperature and distance, researchers can tune the surface morphology, which significantly improves the crystalline quality of subsequent layers like BiFeO3.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the mechanism behind the self-organized formation of step-terrace structures in SrRuO3 thin films grown on mixed-terminated SrTiO3 substrates and how to tune this morphology.

**Main result.** The study identifies a thickness-driven growth-mode transition from 3D island growth to step-flow growth, where the terrace width can be systematically tuned via substrate temperature and target-substrate distance.

**Method.** Pulsed Laser Deposition (PLD) was used for film growth, with surface morphology characterized by Atomic Force Microscopy (AFM), crystal structure analyzed via X-ray Diffraction (XRD) Reciprocal Space Mapping, and ferroelectric domains observed via Piezoelectric Force Microscopy (PFM).

**Model / system.** The experimental platform consists of SrRuO3 (SRO) thin films grown on mixed-terminated SrTiO3 (100) substrates using PLD, with subsequent layers of BiFeO3 (BFO) used to demonstrate the utility of the template.

**Key observables.** Average terrace width, step height, RMS roughness, crystal structure (c-axis alignment), and ferroelectric domain structure (71-degree domains).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Substrate temperature (700-725 C), target-substrate distance (4-4.75 cm), oxygen pressure (80-120 mTorr), and film thickness.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The self-organized structure is assumed to be an intrinsic feature of the growth process rather than inherited from the substrate preparation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares the morphology of as-received vs. annealed STO substrates; Figure 2 shows the AFM surface morphology and line profiles of SRO films.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by establishing the substrate condition, describes the growth-mode transition from 3D islands to step-flow as thickness increases, details the tuning of terrace width via temperature and distance, and concludes by demonstrating improved BFO film quality on the engineered SRO template.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Surface morphology of the substrate and bottom layers plays a critical role in the epitaxial growth of oxide thin films. Here, we report on the self-organized formation of a step-terrace structure in SrRuO3 (SRO) thin films grown using pulsed laser deposition on mixed-terminated SrTiO3 (100) substrates without any prior surface treatment. Atomic force microscopy observations reveal that SRO films initially grow in a three-dimensional island mode and subsequently undergo a transition to a step-flow growth mode through island coalescence as the film thickness increases, resulting in a well-defined step-terrace morphology with a step height consistent with the SRO unit-cell parameter. The average terrace width of the self-organized structure can be systematically tuned by varying the substrate temperature and the target-substrate distance, which we attribute to changes in the critical island radius that governs the nucleation behavior. To demonstrate the utility of this self-organized morphology, we show that BiFeO3 thin films grown on SRO films with such a step-terrace structure exhibit improved surface flatness and crystalline quality compared to those grown directly on bare SrTiO3 substrates. These findings provide a clear understanding of the mechanism of thickness-driven growth-mode transitions in perovskite oxide thin films under various growth conditions.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a unified theoretical description of how surface states evolve through various topological phases in strained semiconductors. It specifically highlights how the bulk nodal line creates a unique non-analytic signature in the surface-state dispersion and spin texture.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the evolution of surface states and their non-analytic properties across topological phase boundaries in strained, spin-orbit coupled semiconductors.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate a unified picture of surface state evolution from 3D topological insulators to Weyl semimetals and reveal a topologically enforced non-analyticity (a 'kink' in dispersion) at the projected nodal line.

**Method.** The work uses an analytical approach based on a minimalistic Luttinger Hamiltonian model, incorporating strain via the Bir-Pikus Hamiltonian and spin-orbit coupling via Dresselhaus terms.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of inverted-band-gap semiconductors like HgTe or alpha-Sn under strain, modeled using an extended Luttinger Hamiltonian that includes strain and bulk inversion asymmetry (BIA) terms.

**Key observables.** Surface-state energy dispersion, spin textures in momentum space, projected density of states (PDOS), and van Hove singularities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Strain magnitude (tensile vs. compressive), spin-orbit coupling strength, Luttinger parameters, and the hierarchy of energy scales (e.g., 10 meV for Dirac physics vs. 3 microeV for Weyl physics).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis utilizes the spherical approximation (gamma3 = gamma2) for certain calculations and assumes the preservation of pseudospin orthogonality via time-reversal symmetry.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the interface geometry and energy diagrams of the inverted band structure; Figure 4 illustrates surface-state dispersion branches and the emergence of secondary Dirac points along high-symmetry directions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the topological phase diagram under strain, develops the mathematical framework using the Luttinger Hamiltonian, derives analytical solutions for surface states, and analyzes the resulting non-analyticities and density of states.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This work explores the topological phase diagram of inverted-band-gap semiconductors under strain and spin-orbit coupling. Using a minimalistic Luttinger Hamiltonian model, we follow the transitions between a 3D topological insulator, a Dirac semimetal, a nodal-line semimetal, and a Weyl semimetal. Analytical and exact solutions for surface states are derived for high-symmetry directions as well as in several limiting cases. We demonstrate the continuous evolution of these surface states across phase boundaries, providing a unified picture that synthesizes previous literature. Specifically, we detail the progression from a Dirac to a nodal-line and then to a Weyl semimetal as spin-orbit coupling originating from bulk inversion asymmetry is introduced. A hierarchy of energy scales is established, defining the criteria for realizing these phases. Finally, we reveal a non-analyticity in the surface-state dispersion at the projected nodal line, originating from distinct, terminating patches of surface states with unique spin textures in momentum space.

</details>
